The Hadith of Four Requisites of Faith and Its Qur'anic Order
Citing a hadith and showing its order matches the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith listing four essential beliefs mirrors the order of topics in Surat al-Baqarah, reinforcing its significance.

Core Idea
The Prophet's hadith on faith follows the Qur'an's own sequence: tawhid, prophethood, resurrection, decree.

This lesson presents a second proof from hadith, showing that the four beliefs necessary for complete faith are arranged in an order that matches the beginning of Surat al-Baqarah. Imam al-Razi demonstrates that the sequence is not arbitrary but divinely patterned.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage of the hadith and its translation. Notice the four beliefs listed: tawhid, prophethood, resurrection, and divine decree.
Some narrations add 'its good and its evil' for decree.
2
Identify the four beliefs
List the four beliefs explicitly mentioned in the hadith. These are the requisites for complete faith according to this prophetic tradition.
3
Study al-Razi's commentary
Read al-Razi's explanation that this order agrees with the Book of God. He maps each belief to a verse from Surat al-Baqarah in the same sequence.
The verses are: [2:21], [2:23], [2:25], [2:26].
4
Reflect on the correspondence
Notice that the hadith's order and the Qur'anic order are identical. This shows the internal coherence of divine revelation.
The Qur'an first distinguishes three groups, then presents proofs in the same order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : روى أبو داود في سننه ، بإسناده ، عن علي بن أبي طالب قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا يؤمن عبد ، حتى يؤمن بأربع : يشهد أن لا إله إلا الله . وأني رسول الله بعثني بالحق. ويؤمن بالبعث بعد الموت . ويؤمن بالقدر ، وزاد بعضهم : ( خيره وشره ) . قال الإمام الداعي إلى الله ، فخر الملة والدين ، محمد الرازي ، ختم له بالحسنى : هذا الترتيب موافق لكتاب الله . وذلك لأنه تعالى فصل أحوال الفرق الثلاثة في أول سورة البقرة . وهم المؤمنون والكافرون والمنافقون . ثم ابتدأ بدلائل التوحيد ، فقال : ( اعبدوا ربكم الذي خلقكم ) ثم ثني بدلائل النبوة فقال : ( وإن كنتم في ريب مما نزلنا ) ثم ثلث بأمر البعث والقيامة ، فقال : ( وبشر الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات : أن لهم جنات ) ثم ربع بأمر القدر ، فقال : ( يضل به كثيراً ، ويهدي به كثيراً ) فصارت الأمور الأربعة المذكورة في الخبر ، هي الأمور المذكورة في أول كتاب الله تعالى بذلك الترتيب من غير فرق .
The second proof: Abu Dawud narrated in his Sunan, with his chain, from 'Ali ibn Abi Talib who said: The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'A servant does not believe until he believes in four things: he bears witness that there is no god but God, and that I am the Messenger of God sent with the truth; and he believes in resurrection after death; and he believes in divine decree (al-qadar).' And some added: 'its good and its evil.' Imam al-Da'i ila Allah, Fakhr al-Milla wa al-Din, Muhammad al-Razi – may his end be good – said: 'This order agrees with the Book of God. That is because God Almighty distinguished the states of the three groups at the beginning of Surat al-Baqarah: the believers, the disbelievers, and the hypocrites. Then He began with proofs of tawhid, saying: 'Worship your Lord who created you' [2:21]; then he followed with proofs of prophethood, saying: 'And if you are in doubt about what We have sent down' [2:23]; then he followed with the matter of resurrection and the Last Day, saying: 'And give good tidings to those who believe and do righteous deeds that they will have gardens' [2:25]; then he followed with the matter of decree, saying: 'He misleads many thereby and guides many thereby' [2:26]. So the four matters mentioned in the report are the matters mentioned at the beginning of the Book of God Almighty in that order without difference.'
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof (al-hujja al-thaniya) which is a hadith narrated by Abu Dawud. The hadith lists four beliefs necessary for complete faith: tawhid, prophethood of Muhammad, resurrection, and divine decree (qadar), with some narrators adding 'its good and its evil.' Imam al-Razi then comments that this order matches the Book of God, specifically the beginning of Surah al-Baqarah. He shows that after distinguishing believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites, the Qur'an sequentially addresses: (1) proofs of tawhid – 'Worship your Lord'; (2) proofs of prophethood – 'If you are in doubt'; (3) resurrection – 'Give good tidings'; (4) decree – 'He misleads many.' Thus the hadith’s order is identical to that of the Qur'an, reinforcing its authenticity and significance.

Key Terms
الحجة الثانية
أربع
القدر
ترتيب
سورة البقرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the hadith is merely listing beliefs without order.
Correction: Al-Razi emphasizes the deliberate sequence mirrors the Qur'an, showing intentional design.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the fourth matter in the hadith and its corresponding Qur'anic verse?
A) Belief in prophets – 'Worship your Lord'
B) Belief in resurrection – 'Give good tidings'
C) Belief in decree – 'He misleads many thereby'
D) Belief in tawhid – 'If you are in doubt'

Key Takeaway
The hadith's order of four beliefs is a precise reflection of the Qur'an's own thematic sequence in Surat al-Baqarah, showing the divine origin of the prophetic teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you personally prioritize these four beliefs in your daily life in the order the Prophet and Qur'an placed them? When you face trials, do you first affirm tawhid, then prophethood, then resurrection, and finally trust in divine decree?
?
If someone asks why believing in qadar is the fourth requisite, not the first, can you defend that order from the Qur'an using this lesson? Does your own approach to faith reflect this Qur'anic sequence?
?
The hadith says 'a servant does not believe until he believes in these four.' How would you react if you realized you have been neglecting one of these, especially the belief in resurrection or decree? What step will you take today to strengthen that weak link?

The Many Meanings of Qadar in Hadith
Arguing that qadar in the hadith means writing, judgment, or knowledge, not creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'qadar' in certain hadiths does not necessarily refer to creation but can also mean writing, judgment, or knowledge, and that this interpretation is supported by linguistic evidence, Qur'anic verses, and a hadith from Abu Hurayra.

Core Idea
Qadar in the hadith is not creation—it is writing, judgment, or knowledge.

This passage comes from a theological discussion on the meaning of qadar in prophetic traditions. The author argues against a common interpretation that qadar means creation, instead showing that the word has multiple valid meanings. By examining linguistic evidence, Qur'anic usage, and a key hadith, he establishes that qadar can refer to divine writing, judgment, or foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Rejection of qadar as creation
Read the opening line where the author explicitly rejects the creation interpretation. Notice how he immediately proposes alternatives.
The author uses 'la nusallim' (we do not accept) to indicate a strong disagreement.
2
Qadar as writing: poetic evidence
Examine the poet's line that uses 'qadara' in the sense of writing in scriptures. Consider how this shows the word's range.
3
Qadar as judgment: Quranic evidence
Look at the two Quranic verses about Lot's wife. The verb 'qadarna' is interpreted as 'we judged', demonstrating another meaning.
The verses are from Surah 15:60 and 27:57.
4
Qadar as knowledge and the hadith proof
Consider the argument that qadar could mean knowledge, especially since God knows all details. Then study the hadith from Abu Hurayra that says 'God's knowledge preceded His creation'.
This hadith is presented as a key evidence that clarifies all reports on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لا نسلم أن المراد بالقدر : الخلق ، بل لفظ القدر ، قد يستعمل أيضاً في الكتابة . قال الشاعر : واعلم : بأن ذا الجلال قد قدر في الصحف الأولى ، التي كان سطر . ويستعمل أيضاً في الحكم. قال تعالى : ( إلا امرأته قدرناها من الغابرين ) وفي آية أخرى : ( قدرنا إنها لمن الغابرين ) أي حكمنا بذلك ، فلم قلتم : إنه ليس المراد من القدر المذكور في هذا الخبر ، هو الكتابة ؟ فإنه تعالى كتب جميع الكائنات في اللوح المحفوظ ، أو نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد منه : الحكم أو العلم . فإنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات على التفصيل التام ؟ والدليل على صحة هذا التأويل : ما روى أبو هريرة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، قال : ( سبق علم الله في خلقه، قبل أن خلقهم. فهم صائرون إلى ما علم الله منهم ) فكان هذا الخبر ، كالمفسر لجميع الأخبار الواردة في باب القدر
When you know this, we say: we do not accept that what is meant by 'qadar' is creation. Rather, the word 'qadar' is also used for writing. The poet said: 'Know that the Majestic One has decreed (qadara) in the first scriptures that were written.' And it is also used for judgment. God Almighty said: 'Except his wife, we decreed (qadarna) her to be among those who remain behind' and in another verse: 'We decreed (qadarna) that she would be among those who remain behind,' meaning we judged thus. So why do you say that what is meant by 'al-qadar' mentioned in this report is not writing? For He Almighty wrote all things that exist in the Preserved Tablet. Or we say: why is it not permissible that what is meant is judgment or knowledge? For He Almighty knows all particulars in complete detail. The evidence for the validity of this interpretation is what Abu Hurayra narrated: that the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'The knowledge of God preceded His creation, before He created them. So they are destined to what God knows of them.' So this report is like an interpretation for all the reports transmitted on the topic of qadar.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. After establishing the hadith's order, the author anticipates a possible interpretation that 'qadar' in the hadith means 'creation.' He rejects this, arguing that 'qadar' has multiple meanings: (1) writing, as in poetry about divine decree in scriptures; (2) judgment, as in the Qur'anic verses about Lot's wife; and (3) knowledge. He asks why it cannot refer to writing (in the Preserved Tablet), judgment, or knowledge, especially since God knows all details. He then provides evidence from a hadith of Abu Hurayra: 'The knowledge of God preceded His creation...' This, he says, clarifies all other reports on qadar. Thus the author defends an interpretation that qadar primarily refers to God's preordained writing, judgment, or knowledge, rather than the act of creation itself.

Key Terms
القدر (qadar)
الخلق (creation)
الكتابة (writing)
الحكم (judgment)
العلم (knowledge)
اللوح المحفوظ (Preserved Tablet)
سبق علم الله (God's knowledge preceded)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'qadar' in hadith always refers to the act of creation.
Correction: The author demonstrates that 'qadar' can mean writing, judgment, or knowledge, and specifically in this hadith it likely refers to God's preordained knowledge, not creation.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main point in this passage?
A) Qadar must mean creation.
B) Qadar can mean writing, judgment, or knowledge, not creation.
C) The hadith of Abu Hurayra is unreliable.
D) The poet's line proves qadar means creation.

Key Takeaway
The hadith on qadar should be understood as referring to God's preordained writing, judgment, or knowledge, not to His act of creation, as shown by linguistic and textual evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe that God's knowledge of it preceded its occurrence? How does this belief either comfort you or challenge your patience?
?
Are there areas of your life where you resist accepting God's judgment (qadar as judgment) because you think you know better? What would it take for you to surrender that resistance today?
?
If qadar in the hadith is not about God creating your actions but about His foreknowledge, how does that affect your sense of personal responsibility? Are you using qadar as an excuse to avoid effort or repentance?

Meaning of Good and Evil in Qadar
Arguing that khayr and sharr mean benefit and harm, not obedience and sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author interprets 'good' and 'evil' in the qadar tradition as worldly benefits and harms rather than moral obedience and sin.

Core Idea
The good and evil of qadar refer to beneficial and harmful creations, not moral acts.

In this passage, the author addresses a key question in predestination theology: what do 'good' and 'evil' mean when we say that everything is from Allah? He argues that they do not necessarily mean moral goodness or sin. Rather, they refer to the worldly blessings and calamities that Allah creates as a test. This lesson unpacks his argument and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concessive opening: 'We grant that...'
2
Identify the concession and main claim
The author starts by granting that qadar means creation. Then he challenges the opponent's interpretation that khayr and sharr refer to obedience and sin. Find this shift.
3
Examine the evidence
The author uses a Quranic verse and Arabic poetry to show that 'evil' often means harm and calamity, not sin. See how this supports his reinterpretation.
The verse 'We test you with evil and good' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن المراد من القدر الخلق والإيجاد . فلم قلتم : إن المراد من الخير والشر، هو الطاعة والمعصية ؟ وذلك لأن لفظ الخير والشر ، قد يستعملان أيضا في المنافع والمضار. قال تعالى : ( ونبلوكم بالشر والخير فتنة ) ومعلوم أن الابتلاء لا يقع بالخير والشر ، بمعنى الطاعة والمعصية . وإنما المراد بالخير : المنافع التي هي الأموال ، والرفاهية في العيش ، والصحة في البدن ، والأهل والأولاد . والمراد بالشر : المضار التي هي القحط والمرض ، والمصيبة . ولهذا سمى الحرب والفتنة : شراً . قال الشاعر : قوم إذا الشر أبدى ناجذيه لهم طاروا إليه زرافات ووحدانا وقال : لكن قومي . وإن كانوا ذوي عدد ليسوا من الشر في شيء ، وإن هانا وقال : فلما أصبح الشر، فأمسى وهو عريان ولم يبق سوى العدوان ، دناهم وفي الشر نجاة حين لا ينجيك إحسان
We grant that what is meant by 'qadar' is creation and bringing into existence. But why do you say that what is meant by 'good' (al-khayr) and 'evil' (al-sharr) is obedience and disobedience? Because the words 'good' and 'evil' are also used for benefits and harms. God Almighty said: 'And We test you with evil and good as a trial' [21:35]. And it is known that testing does not occur with good and evil in the sense of obedience and disobedience. Rather, what is meant by 'good' is benefits which are wealth, ease of living, health of body, family, and children. And what is meant by 'evil' is harms which are drought, illness, and calamity. For this reason, war and sedition are called 'evil.' The poet said: 'A people who, when evil shows its fangs, fly to it in crowds and singly.' And he said: 'But my people, though they are many, are not from evil in anything, even if they are humiliated.' And he said: 'When evil became manifest, and it was naked, and only hostility remained, we approached them, and in evil there is salvation when no kindness can save you.'
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
In this passage, the author concedes for the sake of argument that 'qadar' means creation. He then challenges the interpretation that 'khayr' (good) and 'sharr' (evil) in the phrase 'good and evil of qadar' refer to moral obedience and sin. He argues that these words can also mean worldly benefits and harms. Citing the Qur'anic verse 'And We test you with evil and good as a trial,' he notes that testing involves hardships and ease, not moral categories. He defines 'good' as wealth, comfort, health, family, and 'evil' as drought, illness, calamity. To support this, he quotes Arabic poetry that uses 'sharr' to mean war and conflict. Thus, even if qadar is creation, the good and evil in it are created beneficial and harmful things, not created moral acts. This refines the understanding of the hadith to avoid the problematic implication that God creates sin.

Key Terms
الخير (good)
الشر (evil)
المنافع (benefits)
المضار (harms)
الابتلاء (testing)
الطاعة (obedience)
المعصية (disobedience)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'good and evil of qadar' directly refers to moral actions (obedience and sin).
Correction: The author shows that these terms often refer to worldly benefits and harms, as seen in the Quran and poetry.

Checkpoint
According to the author, 'good' primarily signifies what?
A) Obedience to God
B) Wealth, health, and family
C) Spiritual reward
D) The act of doing good deeds

Key Takeaway
The terms 'good' and 'evil' in the hadith about qadar refer to worldly benefits and harms, not moral obedience and sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a severe trial like illness or loss, do you automatically interpret it as a punishment for sin? This lesson teaches that such 'evil' is a test, not a moral judgment. How might this shift your response from guilt to patience and reliance on Allah? Commit to reciting the verse 'We test you with evil and good as a trial' the next time you face hardship.
?
Think of a time when you experienced a worldly 'good'—wealth, health, or family—and it made you spiritually complacent. Realizing that this good is also a test (fitna) from Allah, how will you use that blessing to draw closer to Him rather than away? What specific action will you take today to turn your blessings into gratitude?
?
This passage argues that the 'evil' in qadar refers to harmful events, not sinful actions. Are there past events in your life that you blamed yourself for (thinking they were caused by your sin) that might actually be tests from Allah? How does this distinction free you from misplaced guilt and enable you to focus on responding with sabr and tawakkul?

The Condition of Qadar: Refuting Dualists
Explaining why belief in qadar's good and evil is necessary to refute dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in qadar (its good and evil) was made a condition of faith by the Prophet to refute the dualist doctrine of two creators.

Core Idea
Belief in qadar refutes dualism by affirming one Creator for all good and evil.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explains why the Prophet (peace be upon him) included belief in qadar (its good and evil) as a condition for faith. The author connects this condition directly to refuting the dualists who claimed two separate creators for pleasure and pain. Understanding this historical context is essential to grasp the theological significance of qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the literal meaning. Note the key terms.
2
Identify the dualist claim
The passage describes dualists as affirming two makers: one for pleasures, one for pains. This is the view being refuted.
3
Connect to the condition of faith
The Prophet made belief in qadar a condition for faith precisely to reject the idea of two independent creators. This shows that qadar is a tawheed issue.
The passage ends mid-sentence but its intent is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : المراد بالقدر خيره وشره: ما ذكرناه . وذلك لأن الثنوية أثبتوا للعالم صانعين . أحدهما : يفعل اللذات والراحات . والآخر : يفعل الآلام والأسقام ، فشرط النبي عليه السلام في الإيمان : حصول الإيمان
When this is established, we say: what is meant by 'qadar, its good and its evil' is what we have mentioned. That is because the dualists (al-thanawiyya) affirmed for the world two makers: one who produces pleasures and comforts, and the other who produces pains and illnesses. So the Prophet, peace be upon him, made belief in qadar a condition for faith: the attainment of faith.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage concludes the argument. After establishing the correct interpretation of 'qadar' and 'good and evil' (as writing/judgment/knowledge, and worldly benefits/harms), the author explains the purpose of the hadith's inclusion of qadar. He refers to the dualists (al-thanawiyya), who believed in two creators – one for good/pleasure and one for evil/pain. The Prophet's condition of believing in qadar (its good and evil from one God) directly refutes this dualist position. Thus, the necessity of belief in qadar is not about affirming that God creates sin, but about affirming that all good and evil – understood as worldly benefit and harm – come from one single Creator. The passage ends mid-sentence (due to page break), but the core point is clear: qadar belief is an anti-dualist stance.

Key Terms
الثنوية (dualists)
صانعين (two makers)
اللذات (pleasures)
الآلام (pains)
شرط (condition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in qadar is only about predestination and divine decree.
Correction: This passage shows that belief in qadar (good and evil from one God) was made a condition of faith to refute dualists who believed in two creators, emphasizing its role in affirming tawheed.

Checkpoint
Why did the Prophet make belief in qadar a condition for faith, according to this passage?
A) To emphasize human free will
B) To refute the dualists who claimed two creators
C) To prove that God creates evil directly
D) To encourage people to accept suffering

Key Takeaway
Belief in qadar (its good and evil) is a condition for faith because it affirms that all good and evil come from one Creator, refuting the dualist notion of two creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience pain or illness, does your heart subtly attribute it to a source other than Allah, as if it came from a different 'maker'? How does this lesson's refutation of dualism challenge you to realign your heart's attribution of all things to Allah alone?
?
Do you truly believe that both the good and the bad in your life come from the same Loving and Wise Creator? If so, why do you sometimes complain about the bad as if it were unjust or out of place? What would change if you saw every trial as part of the same divine decree as every blessing?
?
The Prophet made belief in qadar—both its good and evil—a condition for faith. Examine your own faith: when a major difficulty strikes, does your belief in Allah's oneness remain intact, or do you find yourself questioning His goodness? What concrete step will you take this week to deepen your conviction that all is from Him alone, so that your faith remains unshaken in hardship?

The First Objection: Qadar as Writing or Knowledge
The opponent presents three alternative meanings for 'qadar' and supports them with a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that the term 'qadar' in the hadith may refer to writing in the Preserved Tablet or divine knowledge, not solely to creation, and the evidence from the hadith of Abu Huraira.

Core Idea
Qadar is not only creation; it can mean God's writing or knowledge.

Before defending his own position, the author presents challenges from opponents. The first objection argues that the word 'qadar' in the pivotal hadith could be interpreted as writing (kitabah) in the Preserved Tablet, or judgment (hukm), or knowledge ('ilm), rather than exclusively as creation. The opponent supports this with a hadith of Abu Huraira that emphasizes God's prior knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the opponent's line of reasoning. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the alternative interpretations
The opponent suggests three alternatives to 'creation': writing, judgment, and knowledge. Note how each is introduced and supported.
The evidence from Abu Huraira specifically supports the knowledge interpretation.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The opponent cites a hadith: 'God's knowledge preceded His creation...' Understand how this is meant to interpret all qadar reports.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the opponent would propose these alternatives. What is at stake in defining qadar narrowly or broadly?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بذلك ، فلم قلتم : إنه ليس المراد من القدر المذكور في هذا الخبر ، هو الكتابة ؟ فإنه تعالى كتب جميع الكائنات في اللوح المحفوظ ، أو نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد منه : الحكم أو العلم . فإنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات على التفصيل التام ؟
والدليل على صحة هذا التأويل : ما روى أبو هريرة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، قال : ( سبق علم الله في خلقه، قبل أن خلقهم. فهم صائرون إلى ما علم الله منهم ) فكان هذا الخبر ، كالمفسر لجميع الأخبار الواردة في باب القدر
سلمنا : أن المراد من القدر الخلق والإيجاد . فلم قلتم : إن المراد من الخير والشر، هو الطاعة والمعصية ؟ وذلك لأن لفظ الخير والشر ، قد يستعملان أيضا في المنافع والمضار. قال تعالى : ( ونبلوكم بالشر والخير فتنة ) ومعلوم أن الابتلاء لا يقع بالخير والشر ، بمعنى الطاعة والمعصية . وإنما المراد بالخير : المنافع التي هي الأموال ، والرفاهية في العيش ، والصحة في البدن ، والأهل والأولاد . والمراد بالشر : المضار التي هي القحط والمرض ، والمصيبة . ولهذا سمى الحرب والفتنة : شراً . قال الشاعر : قوم إذا الشر أبدى ناجذيه لهم طاروا إليه زرافات ووحدانا
وقال : لكن قومي . وإن كانوا ذوي عدد ليسوا من الشر في شيء ، وإن هانا
وقال : فلما أصبح الشر، فأمسى وهو عريان ولم يبق سوى العدوان ، دناهم وفي الشر نجاة حين لا ينجيك إحسان
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : المراد بالقدر خيره وشره: ما ذكرناه . وذلك لأن الثنوية أثبتوا للعالم صانعين . أحدهما : يفعل اللذات والراحات . والآخر : يفعل الآلام والأسقام ، فشرط النبي عليه السلام في الإيمان : حصول الإيمان ، بأن فاعل الكل ، هو الله - سبحانه -
سلمنا : أن المراد من الخير والشر : الطاعة والمعصية . إلا أن قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره، ليس فيه بيان أن المعتبر هو الإيمان ، بأن قدر الخير والشر من الله ، أو من العبد . فنحن نقول : المراد : أن قدر الطاعات والمعاصي من العبد ، وأنتم تقولون : المراد : أنه من الله . فلم كان قولكم أولى من قولنا ؟ والذي يؤكد قولنا: أنكم تزعمون أن القدرية هم المعتزلة ، لأجل أنهم أثبتوا القدر لأنفسهم ، ونحن نقول : القدرية هم المجبرة ، لأنهم أثبتوا القدر الله . فعند هذا قلتم : إن تسمية من ثبت القدر لنفسه بالقدري ، أولى من تسمية من ثبت القدر لغيره بالقدري. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك . فاقبلوا ههنا مثل هذا الكلام . وهو أن نقول : حمل قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره ) على إثبات القدر للنفس ، أولى من حمله على إثبات القدر للغير .
وأيضاً : فالأركان الثلاثة المتقدمة. وهي إثبات الربوبية ، وإثبات النبوة ، وإثبات المعاد ، لا تتم إلا بإثبات العبودية للعباد ، وذلك لا يتم إلا أن يكون تقدير الطاعات والمعاصي ، بحسب اختيار العباد . فلما ذكر الأركان الثلاثة ، ثم ذكر عقيبها قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره ) وجب حمله على ما ذكرناه .
سلمنا : ما ذكرتم ، لكن راوي هذا الخبر ، هو علي بن أبي طالب . والقول بالعدل متواتر عنه ، وعن أولاده. والراوي إذا خالف روايته ، أورث ذلك طعناً وضعفاً في الرواية . والله أعلم .
والجواب :
أما حمل القدر على الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ . فجوابه من وجهين :
الأول : إن هذا باطل . لأن المسلمين أجمعوا على أن العلم بوجود اللوح المحفوظ ، وبأن الله تعالى أحدث فيه رقوماً مخصوصة دالة على أحوال هذا العالم، ليس من شرائط الإيمان ، ولا من واجباته . والخبر الذي ذكرناه يدل
That being the case, why do you say that the intended meaning of 'qadar' (predestination) mentioned in this report is not 'writing' (kitabah)? For He, exalted, wrote all things in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz). Or we say: why is it not permissible that the intended meaning is judgment (hukm) or knowledge ('ilm)? For He, exalted, knows all particulars in complete detail? And the evidence for the correctness of this interpretation is what Abu Huraira reported: that the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'God's knowledge preceded His creation before He created them. So they are proceeding towards what God knows of them.' Thus this report is like an interpreter (mufassir) for all the reports transmitted on the topic of qadar. We concede: that the intended meaning of qadar is creation (khalq) and bringing into existence (ijad). So why do you say that the intended meaning of good (khayr) and evil (sharr) is obedience (ta'a) and disobedience (ma'siya)? That is because the terms 'good' and 'evil' may also be used for benefits (manafi') and harms (madarr). He, exalted, said: 'And We test you with evil and good as a trial' (Quran 21:35). It is known that testing does not occur with good and evil in the sense of obedience and disobedience. Rather, the intended meaning of good is benefits, which are wealth, ease in livelihood, health in the body, family, and children. And the intended meaning of evil is harms, which are drought, disease, and calamity. For this reason, war and chaos are called 'evil'. The poet said: 'A people who, when evil shows its fangs to them, rush to it in crowds and individually.' And he said: 'But my people, even if they are numerous, are not in anything of evil, though it may be easy.' And he said: 'When evil became established, and it became naked, and the only thing left was hostility, we drew near to evil, and in evil there is salvation when kindness cannot save you.' If this is established, we say: the intended meaning of qadar, its good and its evil, is what we mentioned. That is because the dualists (al-thanawiyya) affirmed for the world two makers: one who creates pleasures and comforts, and the other who creates pains and illnesses. So the Prophet, peace be upon him, conditioned for faith the attainment of faith that the agent of all is God—glorified be He. We concede: that the intended meaning of good and evil is obedience and disobedience. However, His saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' does not contain a clarification whether what is considered is faith that the determination of good and evil is from God or from the servant. We say: the intended meaning is that the determination of acts of obedience and disobedience is from the servant, and you say: the intended meaning is that it is from God. So why is your statement more preferable than ours? And what confirms our statement is that you claim that the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila because they ascribed qadar to themselves, while we say: the Qadariyya are the Jabriyya because they ascribed qadar to God. So when this is the case, then accept here similar speech. That is, we say: interpreting his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' as affirming qadar for oneself is more appropriate than interpreting it as affirming qadar for another. Also: the three previously mentioned pillars—namely, establishing lordship (rububiyya), establishing prophethood (nubuwwa), and establishing the resurrection (ma'ad)—are not complete except by establishing servitude ('ubudiyya) for the servants, and that is not complete unless the determination of acts of obedience and disobedience is according to the choice (ikhtiyar) of the servants. So when he mentioned the three pillars, then mentioned after them his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil', it must be interpreted as we mentioned. We concede: what you mentioned, but the narrator of this report is 'Ali ibn Abi Talib. The doctrine of justice ('adl) is widely transmitted from him and from his descendants. If a narrator contradicts his own narration, that causes defect and weakness in the narration. And God knows best. The answer: As for interpreting qadar as writing in the Preserved Tablet, the answer to it is from two aspects: The first: This is false because the Muslims are agreed that knowledge of the existence of the Preserved Tablet and that God, exalted, originated in it specific markings indicating the states of this world is not among the conditions of faith nor among its obligations. And the report we mentioned indicates...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the Ash'ari understanding of qadar (predestination) and begins the author's response. The passage is structured as a dialogue: the opponent raises four objections, each introduced by a concession (sallamna) or a direct challenge, and the author starts the answer at the end. The first objection argues that the term 'qadar' in the relevant hadith could mean writing in the Preserved Tablet, or judgment, or knowledge, not necessarily creation. The opponent provides evidence from a hadith of Abu Huraira that God's knowledge preceded creation, claiming this interprets all other qadar reports. The opponent then concedes (sallamna) that qadar means creation but challenges that 'good' and 'evil' may refer to worldly benefits and harms, not obedience and disobedience, citing Quran 21:35 and poetic verses. The opponent then uses this to assert that qadar in the hadith means what they said, referencing the dualists to highlight the necessity of attributing all acts to God alone. The opponent then concedes the meanings of good and evil but argues that the hadith does not specify whether the power of determination belongs to God or the servant, and provides an argument about the naming of Qadariyya and the necessity of the three pillars (lordship, prophethood, resurrection) requiring human choice. Finally, the opponent objects that the narrator is 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, known for the doctrine of justice, so if his narration contradicts his own belief, it weakens the narration. The author's answer begins by addressing the first interpretation of qadar as writing: he states it is false because belief in the Preserved Tablet is not a condition of faith, and the hadith cited actually supports his view (though the text cuts off before completing the second aspect). The author's argument follows the opponent's order, replying to each point as it arises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that 'qadar' in the hadith exclusively refers to creation, ignoring other possible meanings.
Correction: The opponent shows that qadar can also mean writing, judgment, or knowledge, supported by a prophetic hadith.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, which of the following is NOT suggested as an alternative meaning of 'qadar'?
A) Writing in the Preserved Tablet
B) Judgment
C) Human free will
D) Knowledge

Key Takeaway
The first objection expands the possible meanings of qadar through alternative interpretations and a supporting hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that God's knowledge preceded your existence—every detail of your life was known to Him before you were born. Does that truth fill you with trust in His plan, or does it make you feel trapped? What specific fear keeps you from fully surrendering to His decree?
?
The opponent suggests that qadar could mean writing, judgment, or knowledge—not just creation. Have you ever limited your understanding of divine decree to only one aspect (like fate), ignoring that it also encompasses God's deep knowledge and wisdom? How might this limitation affect your patience in trials or your gratitude in blessings?
?
Let this hadith sink in: 'So they are proceeding towards what God knows of them.' Your present struggles and future joys are already known to Him. If you truly believed that, what decision would you make today that you have been postponing out fear or doubt?

Divine Decree
intermediate
7 min
The Second Objection: Good and Evil as Worldly Benefits and Harms
The opponent concedes qadar as creation but challenges the moral interpretation of good and evil, and links it to tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that the terms 'good' (khayr) and 'evil' (sharr) in the qadar hadith can refer to worldly benefits and harms, not exclusively to obedience and disobedience, and how this relates to refuting dualist theology.

Core Idea
Good and evil in the hadith may mean worldly benefits and harms, not just moral actions.

Having conceded that qadar might mean creation, the opponent now challenges the pairing of 'good and evil' with 'obedience and disobedience'. He argues that the words can also mean worldly benefits and harms, citing a Quranic verse and Arabic poetry. He then connects this to the doctrine of tawhid against dualist claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the concession
The opponent starts with 'sallamna' (we concede) but then immediately questions the next step. Identify what is conceded and what is challenged.
2
Examine the linguistic evidence
The opponent uses Quran 21:35 and three lines of poetry to show that 'good' and 'evil' can mean worldly benefits and harms. List the examples.
3
Understand the dualist reference
The opponent brings up the dualists (al-thanawiyya) who believe in two creators. Note how this supports the argument that attributing both good and evil to one God is essential.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider why the opponent uses dualists—what does this imply about the Ash'ari position?

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the Ash'ari understanding of qadar (predestination) and begins the author's response. The passage is structured as a dialogue: the opponent raises four objections, each introduced by a concession (sallamna) or a direct challenge, and the author starts the answer at the end. The opponent then uses this to assert that qadar in the hadith means what they said, referencing the dualists to highlight the necessity of attributing all acts to God alone. The opponent then concedes the meanings of good and evil but argues that the hadith does not specify whether the power of determination belongs to God or the servant, and provides an argument about the naming of Qadariyya and the necessity of the three pillars (lordship, prophethood, resurrection) requiring human choice. The author's argument follows the opponent's order, replying to each point as it arises.

Correction: The opponent demonstrates that these terms can also refer to worldly benefits and harms, as supported by Quranic and poetic usage.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the opponent use to show that 'good' and 'evil' can mean worldly benefits and harms?
A) A verse from the Quran and poetic examples
B) Only the Quran
C) Only poetry
D) Logical deduction

Key Takeaway
Good and evil in the qadar hadith need not be limited to moral categories; they can encompass worldly benefits and harms, reinforcing that all is from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a severe trial—such as illness, loss, or financial hardship—do you truly believe in your heart that it is from Allah alone? Or do you find yourself silently blaming people, circumstances, or even feeling that Allah is unfair? How can you train your soul to receive both good and bad from His hand with equal submission?
?
The opponent uses a Quranic verse to argue that 'evil' can mean worldly harm. Think of a recent 'evil' that befell you. Did you experience it as a test from Allah, or did you see it as meaningless suffering? What would change in your response if you consistently viewed every difficulty as a divinely appointed trial?
?
The hadith declares that both the good and evil of qadar are from Allah. Is there any part of your life where you resist this truth—perhaps by attributing your successes to your own effort and your failures to external factors? What would it look like to surrender that area completely to Allah's decree?

The Third Objection: Who Attributes Qadar to Whom?
The opponent argues that the hadith is ambiguous about the agent of qadar and provides two arguments for human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that the hadith does not specify whether qadar (determination) comes from God or the servant, and their counter-argument based on the naming of Qadariyya and the necessity of human choice for the three pillars of faith.

Core Idea
The hadith leaves open whether qadar is from God or the servant, and the opponent argues it is from the servant.

After further concession, the opponent now focuses on the phrase 'believe in qadar, its good and its evil'. He argues that the hadith does not clarify whether the determination of good and evil is from God or from the human servant. He then presents two arguments: one based on the conventional naming of the Qadariyya sect, and another on the logical necessity of human choice for the three pillars (lordship, prophethood, resurrection).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the concession and question
The opponent concedes the moral interpretation but then points out ambiguity. Identify the central question.
2
Analyze the naming argument
The opponent reverses the usual labels: he says the real Qadariyya are those who attribute qadar to God, while Mu'tazila attribute it to themselves. Understand the logic.
3
Examine the three pillars argument
The opponent argues that the three pillars (rububiyya, nubuwwa, ma'ad) require human servitude ('ubudiyya), which in turn requires free choice. See how this connects to qadar.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical impact
Consider how the opponent turns the Ash'ari position against them by using their own framework (the three pillars mentioned earlier).

بذلك ، فلم قلتم : إنه ليس المراد من القدر المذكور في هذا الخبر ، هو الكتابة ؟ فإنه تعالى كتب جميع الكائنات في اللوح المحفوظ ، أو نقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد منه : الحكم أو العلم . فإنه تعالى عالم بجميع الجزئيات على التفصيل التام ؟
والدليل على صحة هذا التأويل : ما روى أبو هريرة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، قال : ( سبق علم الله في خلقه، قبل أن خلقهم. فهم صائرون إلى ما علم الله منهم ) فكان هذا الخبر ، كالمفسر لجميع الأخبار الواردة في باب القدر
سلمنا : أن المراد من القدر الخلق والإيجاد . فلم قلتم : إن المراد من الخير والشر، هو الطاعة والمعصية ؟ وذلك لأن لفظ الخير والشر ، قد يستعملان أيضا في المنافع والمضار. قال تعالى : ( ونبلوكم بالشر والخير فتنة ) ومعلوم أن الابتلاء لا يقع بالخير والشر ، بمعنى الطاعة والمعصية . وإنما المراد بالخير : المنافع التي هي الأموال ، والرفاهية في العيش ، والصحة في البدن ، والأهل والأولاد . والمراد بالشر : المضار التي هي القحط والمرض ، والمصيبة . ولهذا سمى الحرب والفتنة : شراً . قال الشاعر : قوم إذا الشر أبدى ناجذيه لهم طاروا إليه زرافات ووحدانا
وقال : لكن قومي . وإن كانوا ذوي عدد ليسوا من الشر في شيء ، وإن هانا
وقال : فلما أصبح الشر، فأمسى وهو عريان ولم يبق سوى العدوان ، دناهم وفي الشر نجاة حين لا ينجيك إحسان
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : المراد بالقدر خيره وشره: ما ذكرناه . وذلك لأن الثنوية أثبتوا للعالم صانعين . أحدهما : يفعل اللذات والراحات . والآخر : يفعل الآلام والأسقام ، فشرط النبي عليه السلام في الإيمان : حصول الإيمان ، بأن فاعل الكل ، هو الله - سبحانه -
سلمنا : أن المراد من الخير والشر : الطاعة والمعصية . إلا أن قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره، ليس فيه بيان أن المعتبر هو الإيمان ، بأن قدر الخير والشر من الله ، أو من العبد . فنحن نقول : المراد : أن قدر الطاعات والمعاصي من العبد ، وأنتم تقولون : المراد : أنه من الله . فلم كان قولكم أولى من قولنا ؟ والذي يؤكد قولنا: أنكم تزعمون أن القدرية هم المعتزلة ، لأجل أنهم أثبتوا القدر لأنفسهم ، ونحن نقول : القدرية هم المجبرة ، لأنهم أثبتوا القدر الله . فعند هذا قلتم : إن تسمية من ثبت القدر لنفسه بالقدري ، أولى من تسمية من ثبت القدر لغيره بالقدري. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك . فاقبلوا ههنا مثل هذا الكلام . وهو أن نقول : حمل قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره ) على إثبات القدر للنفس ، أولى من حمله على إثبات القدر للغير .
وأيضاً : فالأركان الثلاثة المتقدمة. وهي إثبات الربوبية ، وإثبات النبوة ، وإثبات المعاد ، لا تتم إلا بإثبات العبودية للعباد ، وذلك لا يتم إلا أن يكون تقدير الطاعات والمعاصي ، بحسب اختيار العباد . فلما ذكر الأركان الثلاثة ، ثم ذكر عقيبها قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره ) وجب حمله على ما ذكرناه .
سلمنا : ما ذكرتم ، لكن راوي هذا الخبر ، هو علي بن أبي طالب . والقول بالعدل متواتر عنه ، وعن أولاده. والراوي إذا خالف روايته ، أورث ذلك طعناً وضعفاً في الرواية . والله أعلم .
والجواب :
أما حمل القدر على الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ . فجوابه من وجهين :
الأول : إن هذا باطل . لأن المسلمين أجمعوا على أن العلم بوجود اللوح المحفوظ ، وبأن الله تعالى أحدث فيه رقوماً مخصوصة دالة على أحوال هذا العالم، ليس من شرائط الإيمان ، ولا من واجباته . والخبر الذي ذكرناه يدل
That being the case, why do you say that the intended meaning of 'qadar' (predestination) mentioned in this report is not 'writing' (kitabah)? For He, exalted, wrote all things in the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz). Or we say: why is it not permissible that the intended meaning is judgment (hukm) or knowledge ('ilm)? For He, exalted, knows all particulars in complete detail? And the evidence for the correctness of this interpretation is what Abu Huraira reported: that the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'God's knowledge preceded His creation before He created them. So they are proceeding towards what God knows of them.' Thus this report is like an interpreter (mufassir) for all the reports transmitted on the topic of qadar. We concede: that the intended meaning of qadar is creation (khalq) and bringing into existence (ijad). So why do you say that the intended meaning of good (khayr) and evil (sharr) is obedience (ta'a) and disobedience (ma'siya)? That is because the terms 'good' and 'evil' may also be used for benefits (manafi') and harms (madarr). He, exalted, said: 'And We test you with evil and good as a trial' (Quran 21:35). It is known that testing does not occur with good and evil in the sense of obedience and disobedience. Rather, the intended meaning of good is benefits, which are wealth, ease in livelihood, health in the body, family, and children. And the intended meaning of evil is harms, which are drought, disease, and calamity. For this reason, war and chaos are called 'evil'. The poet said: 'A people who, when evil shows its fangs to them, rush to it in crowds and individually.' And he said: 'But my people, even if they are numerous, are not in anything of evil, though it may be easy.' And he said: 'When evil became established, and it became naked, and the only thing left was hostility, we drew near to evil, and in evil there is salvation when kindness cannot save you.' If this is established, we say: the intended meaning of qadar, its good and its evil, is what we mentioned. That is because the dualists (al-thanawiyya) affirmed for the world two makers: one who creates pleasures and comforts, and the other who creates pains and illnesses. So the Prophet, peace be upon him, conditioned for faith the attainment of faith that the agent of all is God—glorified be He. We concede: that the intended meaning of good and evil is obedience and disobedience. However, His saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' does not contain a clarification whether what is considered is faith that the determination of good and evil is from God or from the servant. We say: the intended meaning is that the determination of acts of obedience and disobedience is from the servant, and you say: the intended meaning is that it is from God. So why is your statement more preferable than ours? And what confirms our statement is that you claim that the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila because they ascribed qadar to themselves, while we say: the Qadariyya are the Jabriyya because they ascribed qadar to God. So when this is the case, then accept here similar speech. That is, we say: interpreting his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' as affirming qadar for oneself is more appropriate than interpreting it as affirming qadar for another. Also: the three previously mentioned pillars—namely, establishing lordship (rububiyya), establishing prophethood (nubuwwa), and establishing the resurrection (ma'ad)—are not complete except by establishing servitude ('ubudiyya) for the servants, and that is not complete unless the determination of acts of obedience and disobedience is according to the choice (ikhtiyar) of the servants. So when he mentioned the three pillars, then mentioned after them his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil', it must be interpreted as we mentioned. We concede: what you mentioned, but the narrator of this report is 'Ali ibn Abi Talib. The doctrine of justice ('adl) is widely transmitted from him and from his descendants. If a narrator contradicts his own narration, that causes defect and weakness in the narration. And God knows best. The answer: As for interpreting qadar as writing in the Preserved Tablet, the answer to it is from two aspects: The first: This is false because the Muslims are agreed that knowledge of the existence of the Preserved Tablet and that God, exalted, originated in it specific markings indicating the states of this world is not among the conditions of faith nor among its obligations. And the report we mentioned indicates...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the Ash'ari understanding of qadar (predestination) and begins the author's response. The passage is structured as a dialogue: the opponent raises four objections, each introduced by a concession (sallamna) or a direct challenge, and the author starts the answer at the end. The first objection argues that the term 'qadar' in the relevant hadith could mean writing in the Preserved Tablet, or judgment, or knowledge, not necessarily creation. The opponent provides evidence from a hadith of Abu Huraira that God's knowledge preceded creation, claiming this interprets all other qadar reports. The opponent then concedes (sallamna) that qadar means creation but challenges that 'good' and 'evil' may refer to worldly benefits and harms, not obedience and disobedience, citing Quran 21:35 and poetic verses. The opponent then uses this to assert that qadar in the hadith means what they said, referencing the dualists to highlight the necessity of attributing all acts to God alone. The opponent then concedes the meanings of good and evil but argues that the hadith does not specify whether the power of determination belongs to God or the servant, and provides an argument about the naming of Qadariyya and the necessity of the three pillars (lordship, prophethood, resurrection) requiring human choice. Finally, the opponent objects that the narrator is 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, known for the doctrine of justice, so if his narration contradicts his own belief, it weakens the narration. The author's answer begins by addressing the first interpretation of qadar as writing: he states it is false because belief in the Preserved Tablet is not a condition of faith, and the hadith cited actually supports his view (though the text cuts off before completing the second aspect). The author's argument follows the opponent's order, replying to each point as it arises.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the term 'Qadariyya' exclusively refers to those who believe in free will, but the opponent argues it can be reversed.
Correction: The opponent claims that linguistically, 'Qadariyya' could apply to those who ascribe qadar to God, not to themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the opponent, why must the three pillars (rububiyya, nubuwwa, ma'ad) be interpreted as requiring human choice?
A) Because they are incomplete without servitude, which requires choice
B) Because the hadith says so
C) Because the Quran explicitly states it
D) Because of consensus

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the hadith's ambiguity and the structure of the three pillars support attributing qadar to human choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponent argues that the three pillars of faith require you to have genuine choice in your actions. Examine your own heart: Do you truly take responsibility for your obedience and disobedience, or do you sometimes blame God's decree for your failures? What would change in your spiritual life today if you fully owned every choice as your own?
?
The opponent claims that attributing qadar to God makes Him worthy of blame for evil. Have you ever felt resentment toward God when things went wrong, as if He had forced you to sin? This lesson calls you to examine whether you are using divine decree as a shield against repentance. Ask yourself: Am I willing to stand before God and take full accountability for every sin, without shifting blame to His decree?
?
The opponent reinterprets the meaning of 'Qadariyya' to defend human agency. This shows how beliefs can be shaped by labels and definitions. Think about the beliefs you hold about free will and predestination: Are they rooted in defending yourself from responsibility, or are they genuinely oriented toward serving God? What concrete change in your daily actions would prove your belief is sincere?

The Fourth Objection and the Beginning of the Author's Response
The opponent raises an objection about the narrator 'Ali, and the author begins his response focusing on the first interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection based on the narrator 'Ali's known stance on justice, and the author's initial response that the interpretation of qadar as writing in the Preserved Tablet is not required for faith.

Core Idea
Even if 'Ali narrated the hadith, his known stance on justice does not invalidate the Ash'ari interpretation, as faith does not require belief in the Preserved Tablet.

The opponent's final objection points out that the hadith was narrated by 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, who is known for his strong emphasis on divine justice ('adl). The opponent claims that if 'Ali's narration contradicts his own doctrine, it weakens the hadith. The author begins his answer by addressing the first objection (qadar as writing) and argues that belief in the Preserved Tablet is not a condition of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth objection
Identify the opponent's claim about 'Ali and his doctrine of justice. Understand why this is presented as a problem.
2
Read the beginning of the author's answer
The author starts with the first objection about writing in the Preserved Tablet. Note his first argument.
3
Understand the author's reasoning
The author says that belief in the Preserved Tablet and its inscriptions is not a condition of faith. So interpreting qadar as such would make faith dependent on something unnecessary.
4
Reflect on the incomplete answer
The passage cuts off before the author completes the second aspect and the answers to other objections. Consider how this affects the overall argument.

However, His saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' does not contain a clarification whether what is considered is faith that the determination of good and evil is from God or from the servant. We say: the intended meaning is that the determination of acts of obedience and disobedience is from the servant, and you say: the intended meaning is that it is from God. So why is your statement more preferable than ours? And what confirms our statement is that you claim that the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila because they ascribed qadar to themselves, while we say: the Qadariyya are the Jabriyya because they ascribed qadar to God. So when this is the case, then accept here similar speech. That is, we say: interpreting his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' as affirming qadar for oneself is more appropriate than interpreting it as affirming qadar for another. Also: the three previously mentioned pillars—namely, establishing lordship (rububiyya), establishing prophethood (nubuwwa), and establishing the resurrection (ma'ad)—are not complete except by establishing servitude ('ubudiyya) for the servants, and that is not complete unless the determination of acts of obedience and disobedience is according to the choice (ikhtiyar) of the servants. So when he mentioned the three pillars, then mentioned after them his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil', it must be interpreted as we mentioned. We concede: what you mentioned, but the narrator of this report is 'Ali ibn Abi Talib. The doctrine of justice ('adl) is widely transmitted from him and from his descendants. If a narrator contradicts his own narration, that causes defect and weakness in the narration. And God knows best. The answer: As for interpreting qadar as writing in the Preserved Tablet, the answer to it is from two aspects: The first: This is false because the Muslims are agreed that knowledge of the existence of the Preserved Tablet and that God, exalted, originated in it specific markings indicating the states of this world is not among the conditions of faith nor among its obligations. And the report we mentioned indicates...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections against the Ash'ari understanding of qadar (predestination) and begins the author's response. The passage is structured as a dialogue: the opponent raises four objections, each introduced by a concession (sallamna) or a direct challenge, and the author starts the answer at the end. The first objection argues that the term 'qadar' in the relevant hadith could mean writing in the Preserved Tablet, or judgment, or knowledge, not necessarily creation. The opponent provides evidence from a hadith of Abu Huraira that God's knowledge preceded creation, claiming this interprets all other qadar reports. The opponent then concedes (sallamna) that qadar means creation but challenges that 'good' and 'evil' may refer to worldly benefits and harms, not obedience and disobedience, citing Quran 21:35 and poetic verses. The opponent then uses this to assert that qadar in the hadith means what they said, referencing the dualists to highlight the necessity of attributing all acts to God alone. The opponent then concedes the meanings of good and evil but argues that the hadith does not specify whether the power of determination belongs to God or the servant, and provides an argument about the naming of Qadariyya and the necessity of the three pillars (lordship, prophethood, resurrection) requiring human choice. Finally, the opponent objects that the narrator is 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, known for the doctrine of justice, so if his narration contradicts his own belief, it weakens the narration. The author's answer begins by addressing the first interpretation of qadar as writing: he states it is false because belief in the Preserved Tablet is not a condition of faith, and the hadith cited actually supports his view (though the text cuts off before completing the second aspect). The author's argument follows the opponent's order, replying to each point as it arises.

علي بن أبي طالب
العدل
اللوح المحفوظ
الكتابة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'Ali's narration of a hadith that appears predestinarian necessarily conflicts with his known stance on justice.
Correction: The author implies that the hadith does not conflict with 'Ali's stance; the interpretation is the issue. He begins to show that the alternate interpretation is not required for faith.

The author begins to refute the objections by showing that the proposed alternative (qadar as writing) is not essential for faith, thus defusing the opponent's challenge.

The Qadar Debate: Attributing Power to God or Servant?
Objection and counter-argument using sectarian naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the labeling of the Qadariyya sect to argue that the hadith about qadar should be interpreted as attributing qadar to the servant rather than to God.

Core Idea
The hadith's ambiguity is resolved by who is called Qadariyya—those who claim qadar for themselves.

This passage captures a pivotal debate in Islamic theology about the meaning of qadar in a famous hadith. The author presents an objection and then turns the tables using the very naming of the Qadariyya sect. By examining who is called Qadariyya, the author argues that the hadith must mean attributing qadar to the servant, not to God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector concedes that good/evil mean obedience/disobedience but argues the hadith is ambiguous about whether qadar is from God or the servant.
Note the concession: 'good and evil' are defined as obedience and disobedience.
2
Notice the author's counter
The author invokes the naming of the Qadariyya: Mu'tazila are called Qadariyya for attributing qadar to themselves, while Jabriyya attribute qadar to God.
This is a key move: using sectarian labels as evidence.
3
Follow the logic
The objector earlier claimed naming after oneself is more appropriate. The author uses that same principle to argue that interpreting the hadith as attributing qadar to the self (servant) is more fitting.
The author turns the objector's own criterion against him.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, the hadith should be interpreted as attributing qadar to the servant, not to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا : أن المراد من الخير والشر : الطاعة والمعصية . إلا أن قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره، ليس فيه بيان أن المعتبر هو الإيمان ، بأن قدر الخير والشر من الله ، أو من العبد . فنحن نقول : المراد : أن قدر الطاعات والمعاصي من العبد ، وأنتم تقولون : المراد : أنه من الله . فلم كان قولكم أولى من قولنا ؟ والذي يؤكد قولنا: أنكم تزعمون أن القدرية هم المعتزلة ، لأجل أنهم أثبتوا القدر لأنفسهم ، ونحن نقول : القدرية هم المجبرة ، لأنهم أثبتوا القدر الله . فعند هذا قلتم : إن تسمية من ثبت القدر لنفسه بالقدري ، أولى من تسمية من ثبت القدر لغيره بالقدري. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك . فاقبلوا ههنا مثل هذا الكلام . وهو أن نقول : حمل قوليه : « وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره ، على إثبات القدر للنفس ، أولى من جمله على إثبات القدر للغير .
We concede that by good and evil is meant obedience and disobedience. However, his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' does not clarify whether the belief considered is that the measure of good and evil is from God or from the servant. So we say: the intended meaning is that the measure of acts of obedience and disobedience is from the servant, while you say: the intended meaning is that it is from God. So why is your statement more worthy than ours? And what confirms our statement is that you claim that the Qadariyya are the Mu'tazila because they attributed qadar to themselves, while we say: the Qadariyya are the Jabriyya because they attributed qadar to God. So then you said: naming one who attributes qadar to himself as Qadari is more appropriate than naming one who attributes qadar to another as Qadari. And if the matter is so, then accept here such a statement. That is, we say: interpreting his two sayings: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil' as attributing qadar to the self is more appropriate than interpreting it as attributing qadar to another.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a counter-argument regarding the interpretation of the hadith about belief in qadar. The objector concedes that 'good and evil' refer to obedience and disobedience, but argues that the hadith does not specify whether the qadar (measure) of these actions is from God or from the servant. The objector then challenges the author's position by asking why their interpretation (that qadar is from God) is more valid than the objector's own (that qadar is from the servant). The author responds with a rhetorical argument based on the naming of the Qadariyya sect: the Mu'tazila are called Qadariyya because they attribute qadar to themselves (i.e., they affirm human free will), while the Jabriyya attribute qadar to God. The author argues that the objector's own logic—that naming after oneself is more appropriate—actually supports the author's view that the hadith should be interpreted as attributing qadar to the servant (since that aligns with the Mu'tazila labeling). The author then asserts that interpreting the hadith's phrase 'belief in qadar' as attributing qadar to the self is more appropriate than attributing it to another (God).

Correction: The author shows that the naming of the Qadariyya resolves the ambiguity: those who attribute qadar to themselves are called Qadari, so the hadith must mean attribution to the servant.

Checkpoint

C) The Jabriyya are called Qadariyya for attributing qadar to God.

Key Takeaway
The author uses the sectarian label Qadariyya to resolve the ambiguity in the hadith, concluding that qadar should be attributed to the servant, not to God.

The Three Pillars Require Human Choice
Proof that the three pillars require affirming human choice for qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming servitude ('ubudiyya) is necessary for completing the three pillars of faith, and how this connects to the interpretation of qadar as requiring human choice.

Core Idea
The three pillars of faith cannot stand without human choice in actions.

In this passage, the author presents a logical argument linking the three fundamental beliefs (lordship, prophethood, resurrection) to the necessity of human free will. He argues that these pillars require affirming the servant's role, which in turn depends on actions being measured by the servant's own choice. This understanding clarifies why belief in qadar must be interpreted as referring to human responsibility rather than mere divine compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three pillars
Identify the three pillars mentioned: lordship (rububiyya), prophethood (nubuwwa), and resurrection (ma'ad). Recognize they are foundational.
2
Follow the logic of completion
Trace the argument: pillars require servitude, servitude requires choice in actions. See how each step is necessary.
Notice the conditional chain: لا تتم إلا بـ.
3
Connect to qadar interpretation
See why the author uses the hadith's order to support his view that qadar refers to the servant's choice.
The hadith lists qadar right after the pillars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فالأركان الثلاثة المتقدمة. وهي إثبات الربوبية ، وإثبات النبوة ، وإثبات المعاد ، لا تتم إلا بإثبات العبودية للعباد ، وذلك لا يتم إلا أن يكون تقدير الطاعات والمعاصي ، بحسب اختيار العباد . فلما ذكر الأركان الثلاثة ، ثم ذكر عقيبها قوله : ( وأن تؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره ، وجب حمله على ما ذكرناه .
And also: the three preceding pillars—which are affirming lordship, affirming prophethood, and affirming the resurrection—are not completed except by affirming servitude to the servants, and that is not completed except that the measure (taqdir) of acts of obedience and disobedience is according to the choice of the servants. So when he mentioned the three pillars, then after them he mentioned his saying: 'And that you believe in qadar, its good and its evil,' it is necessary to interpret it as we have stated.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage provides a second argument for interpreting qadar as referring to the servant's choice. The author argues that the three fundamental pillars of faith—affirmation of God's lordship, affirmation of prophethood, and affirmation of the resurrection—are not complete without affirming the servitude ('ubudiyya) of the servants. Servitude requires that the actions of obedience and disobedience be measured (taqdir) according to the servant's own choice. Since the hadith lists belief in qadar immediately after these three pillars, it must be understood as belief that qadar (the measure of actions) is from the servant's choice, not from God's direct creation.

Correction: The author argues that the three pillars require human choice, so qadar must involve human responsibility.

Key Takeaway
The affirmation of lordship, prophethood, and resurrection logically demands that humans have free choice in their actions, making qadar a matter of human responsibility.

Refutation of Misinterpretations of Qadar
Refutation of two misinterpretations of qadar using logical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qadar cannot be interpreted as writing in the Preserved Tablet or as divine explanation, using logical arguments from Sunni creed.

Core Idea
Qadar is an obligatory belief, but knowing the Preserved Tablet is not.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah confronts two misinterpretations of qadar. The author uses logical syllogisms to show that if qadar meant writing in the Preserved Tablet or divine explanation, believers would not be required to know it – yet the hadith makes belief in qadar a condition of faith. Thus, those interpretations are invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the author's structure: two distinct refutations.
2
Identify the two refutations
The first refutation targets interpreting qadar as writing in the Preserved Tablet. The second targets interpreting it as divine explanation (bayan).
3
Analyze the logical structure
The author uses a syllogism (second figure) to show a contradiction: belief in qadar is obligatory, but belief in the Preserved Tablet or divine explanation is not, so qadar cannot mean those.
4
Connect to broader creed
Reflect on why this matters: qadar is a core belief, but details like the Tablet's existence are not required for faith.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to consolidate your grasp of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب :
أما حمل القدر على الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ . فجوابه من وجهين :
الأول : إن هذا باطل . لأن المسلمين أجمعوا على أن العلم بوجود اللوح المحفوظ ، وبأن الله تعالى أحدث فيه رقوماً مخصوصة دالة على أحوال هذا العالم، ليس من شرائط الإيمان ، ولا من واجباته . والخبر الذي ذكرناه يدل على أن الإيمان بالقدر من شرائط صحة الإيمان. وهذا ينتج من الشكل الثاني : أنه ليس المراد من القدر المذكور في هذا الخبر هو الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ :
وأما حمل القدر على العلم واللسان ، فباطل أيضاً . لأن ذلك البيان ، إما أن يكون بياناً للبشر ، وهو مفقود . أو للملائكة ، وهو أيضاً باطل . لأن العلم بأن الله بين أحوال أفعال العباد للملائكة ليس من واجبات الإيمان . وإيمان بالقدر من واجبات الإيمان بحكم دلالة هذا الخبر . وذلك يدل على أنه ليس المراد من هذا القدر هو البيان .
The answer: As for interpreting qadar as writing in the Preserved Tablet, its refutation is from two aspects: First, this is invalid because the Muslims have unanimously agreed that knowledge of the existence of the Preserved Tablet and that God the Exalted inscribed in it specific markings indicating the states of this world is not among the conditions of faith nor among its obligations. And the hadith we mentioned indicates that belief in qadar is among the conditions of sound faith. This yields from the second figure that the intended meaning of qadar mentioned in this hadith is not the writing in the Preserved Tablet. As for interpreting qadar as 'al-'ilm (knowledge) and 'al-lisan (the tongue), it is also invalid. Because that explanation (al-bayan) is either an explanation to humans, which is non-existent, or to the angels, and it is also invalid. Because knowledge that God explained the states of the actions of the servants to the angels is not among the obligations of faith. And belief in qadar is among the obligations of faith by virtue of the indication of this hadith. And that indicates that the intended meaning of this qadar is not the explanation.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is part of the author's response to the objection about 'Ali's hadith. The author refutes two interpretations of 'qadar': (1) that it means writing in the Preserved Tablet, and (2) that it means divine explanation (bayan) to humans or angels. For the first, the author gives two reasons: Muslims do not require belief in the Preserved Tablet as a condition of faith, while belief in qadar is required by the hadith; therefore, qadar cannot mean that. For the second, the author argues that if qadar meant God's explanation to humans or angels, such knowledge is not obligatory for faith, yet belief in qadar is obligatory, so it cannot be that meaning. Both refutations use a logical syllogism (the second figure) to show that the interpretation contradicts the obligatory nature of belief in qadar.

شرائط الإيمان
واجبات الإيمان
بيان
شكل ثاني

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in qadar requires belief in the details of the Preserved Tablet or in divine explanations to angels.
Correction: The author shows that these are not obligatory for faith, so qadar must mean something else more fundamental.

Checkpoint

A) The hadith does not mention the Tablet.
B) Belief in the Tablet is not obligatory for faith, but belief in qadar is.

Key Takeaway

Why Qadar Cannot Be Reduced to Divine Knowledge
Refutation of interpreting qadar as any form of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting qadar (divine decree) as mere knowledge leads to logical and theological problems, including compulsion (jabr).

Core Idea
Qadar is not just knowledge; interpreting it as such leads to jabr.

The author examines three possible ways to interpret qadar as knowledge and refutes each. This lesson walks through those refutations, showing why qadar must involve creative power, not merely foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the three conditional statements (إن حمل على...).
2
Identify the three interpretations
Find each interpretation of qadar as knowledge that the author refutes. Underline them in the translation.
They are: any knowledge, knowledge necessitating action, and knowledge of how servants act.
3
Understand the logical consequences
For each interpretation, see why the author says it is invalid or leads to jabr. Ask yourself why each outcome is unacceptable.
The key is that qadar must be a creative act, not just knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حمل القدر على العلم ، فضعيف . لأنه إن حمل على أي علم كان ، فهو باطل . بدليل : أن من علم ذات الله وصفاته . لا يقال : إنه قدر ذات الله وصفاته ، وإن حمل على العلم المقتضي إيقاع الفعل على وجه خاص ، فهذا يقتضي أن لا يحصل القدر إلا مع التخليق والتكوين . وإن حمل على علم الله بكيفية أفعال العباد ، فالجبر أيضاً لازم. لما ثبت أن خلاف معلوم الله : محال الوقوع .
As for interpreting qadar as knowledge, it is weak. Because if it is interpreted as any knowledge whatsoever, it is invalid. The proof is: one who knows God's essence and attributes is not said to have measured (qaddara) God's essence and attributes. And if it is interpreted as knowledge that necessitates the occurrence of the action in a particular manner, then this necessitates that qadar does not occur except with creation (takhlīq) and formation (takwīn). And if it is interpreted as God's knowledge of the modality of the actions of the servants, then jabr (compulsion) is also necessary, because it is established that the occurrence of what contradicts God's known is impossible.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage refutes three possible interpretations of qadar as knowledge. First, if qadar means any knowledge, it is invalid because one does not say that knowledge of God's essence and attributes is 'measuring' them. Second, if it means knowledge that necessitates a specific occurrence, then qadar would only occur with divine creation (takhlīq) and formation (takwīn), which would imply compulsion. Third, if it means God's knowledge of how servants will act, then jabr is necessary because God's knowledge is infallible and cannot be contradicted, leading to compulsion. Thus, none of these interpretations are acceptable.

Key Terms
علم الله
تخليق
تكوين
جبر
معلوم الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qadar is simply God's foreknowledge of what will happen.
Correction: The author shows that this interpretation leads to jabr (compulsion) because it denies human free will and reduces qadar to mere knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the author, interpreting qadar as God's knowledge of how servants will act leads to what?
A) Free will.
B) Compulsion (jabr).
C) Coherence.
D) Affirmation of the Preserved Tablet.

Key Takeaway
Interpreting qadar solely as knowledge leads to logical inconsistencies and ultimately to jabr, showing that qadar must entail divine causal power beyond mere knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by saying 'It was my qadar'? After reading this lesson, how do you see the difference between God's knowledge of your action and His forcing you to act? Will you now take full responsibility for your choices, knowing that qadar is not just foreknowledge but involves divine power without removing your accountability?
?
The author shows that reducing qadar to knowledge leads to logical contradictions. Are there areas of your Islamic understanding where you have accepted an oversimplified explanation without realizing its deeper theological implications? Commit today to studying 'aqīdah with the depth it deserves.
?
Consider the phrase 'God knows what I will do, so I'm not free.' This lesson reveals that such thinking is actually a form of jabr (compulsion). How does this realization change the way you view your own free will? Will you now actively reject fatalistic thoughts and embrace the balance between divine decree and human choice?
