The Two Mainstays: Intuitive Premises
Author states the core intuitive premises of the two sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational intuitive premises that each sect relies on and why they conflict.

Core Idea
Two intuitive premises clash: every contingent needs a cause vs. praise/blame require human power.

The debate over predestination and free will is not merely theological speculation; it rests on intuitive judgments that all humans recognize. These foundational premises are the starting point of the entire discourse. This lesson presents each sect's central tenet as they themselves see it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to get the author's argument.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the statement of the Qadariyya's premise (حسن المدح والذم) and the Jabariyya's premise (الممكن لا بد له من مرجع). Note how each is claimed to be intuitive.
The author says both are known by intuition (بديهة).
3
Understand the conflict
Although both premises are intuitive, they lead to opposite conclusions about human agency. Reflect on how this sets up the entire debate.
This conflict is the central problem the author aims to resolve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القدرية : فقد عولوا في تقرير قولهم : على أن حسن المدح والذم معلوم بالبديهة. ونعلم بالبديهة : أنه لو لم يكن العبد قادراً على الفعل لما حسن المدح والذم . وما كان أصلا للبديهي : أولى أن يكون بديهياً . فالحاصل : أن عمدة الجبرية هي أن الممكن لا بد له من مرجع وعمدة القدرية : هي حسن المدح والذم . وهما مقدمتان معلومتان بالبديهة . فقد وقع التعارض بين هاتين المقدمتين في هذا المقام . وأما الدلائل العقلية : فاعتماد الجبرية على أن هذه التفاصيل ، غير معلومة لنا فلا تكون مخلوقة لنا . واعتماد القدرية على أن هذه الأفعال ، واقعة على وفق مقصودنا ودواعينا . فهي منا . فقد وقع التعارض بين دليل العلم ودليل القصد . وأما الإلزامات العائدة إلى باب الكمال والنقصان . فاعتماد الجبرية على حرف واحد ، وهو أن القدرة على الإيجاد صفة كمال ، فلا يليق بالعبد الذي هو منبع النقصان . واعتماد القدرية على حرف واحد ، وهو أن أفعال العباد سفه وعبث ، وإيجادها نقصان . وذلك لا يليق بالإله المتعالي عن النقصان . وأما الدلائل السمعية . فالقرآن مملوء مما يوهم الجبر تارة ، ومما يوهم القدر أخرى . وأما الأنام . فكذلك جميع الملل والنحل ، بعضهم مجبرة ، وبعضهم قدرية . ولا ترى أمة من الأمم خالية عن هاتين الطائفتين . وأما الأوضاع والحكايات . فهي أيضاً متعارضة في هذا الباب ، حتى قيل : إن واضع النرد وضعه على مذهب الجبر ، وواضع الشطرنج وضعه على مذهب القدر. فظهر عندك أن التعارض غالب في هذه المسألة إلا أن مذهبنا أقوى بسبب حرف واحد ، وهو أنا إن قدحنا في قول : الممكن لا بد له من سبب انسد علينا باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن سلمنا بهذه المقدمة لزمنا القول بأن العبد غير مستقل بالإيجاد . فلما كان دليل قولنا في هذه المسألة ، ودليل إثبات الصانع : دليلاً واحداً ، لا جرم كان جانبنا أقوى وأكمل .
As for the Qadariyya: they relied in establishing their statement on the fact that the goodness of praise and blame is known by intuition. And we know by intuition that if the servant were not capable of the act, then praise and blame would not be good. And what is the foundation of the intuitive is more worthy of being intuitive. So the conclusion: the mainstay of the Jabariyya is that the contingent must have a source, and the mainstay of the Qadariyya is the goodness of praise and blame. And they are two premises known by intuition. Thus conflict occurred between these two premises in this matter. As for rational proofs: the reliance of the Jabariyya is that these details are not known to us, so they are not created by us. And the reliance of the Qadariyya is that these acts occur in accordance with our intention and motives, so they are from us. Thus conflict occurred between the proof of knowledge and the proof of intention. As for the implications that revert to the matter of perfection and deficiency: the reliance of the Jabariyya is on one point, which is that power to bring into existence is an attribute of perfection, so it is not befitting the servant who is a source of deficiency. And the reliance of the Qadariyya is on one point, which is that the acts of servants are folly and frivolity, and bringing them into existence is deficiency, and that is not befitting the God exalted above deficiency. As for scriptural proofs: the Qur'an is filled with what suggests predestination at times and what suggests free will at others. As for the nations: likewise all religious communities and sects, some are Jabariyya, some are Qadariyya. And you do not see any nation that is free of these two groups. As for customs and stories: they also conflict in this matter, until it was said: the inventor of backgammon placed it according to the school of jabr, and the inventor of chess placed it according to the school of qadr. So it became clear to you that conflict is dominant in this issue, except that our school is stronger due to one point: that if we object to the statement 'the contingent must have a cause', the door to proving the Maker is blocked for us, and if we accept this premise, we are compelled to say that the servant is not independent in bringing into existence. So since the proof of our statement in this issue and the proof of establishing the Maker are one proof, inevitably our side is stronger and more complete.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of the arguments between the Jabariyya (predestinarians) and the Qadariyya (free-will advocates) across multiple categories, concluding with the author's defense of the Ash'ari/sunni position. The author begins with the rational intuitive premises: the Jabariyya base their view on the principle that every contingent event requires a source (cause), while the Qadariyya base theirs on the intuitive moral judgment that praise and blame only apply to voluntary acts. These two intuitive premises conflict. Next, rational proofs: the Jabariyya argue that if the details of actions are not known to us, they cannot be created by us (implying they are from God); the Qadariyya argue that acts follow our intentions and motives, so they originate from us. This is a conflict between the proof of knowledge and the proof of intention. Then perfection and deficiency: the Jabariyya say power to create is a perfection, so it belongs only to God, not the deficient servant; the Qadariyya say that creating acts of folly would be a deficiency in God. Scriptural proofs: the Qur'an contains verses that seem to support both sides. Similarly, all nations contain both groups. Even customs and stories reflect the conflict (e.g., the inventors of backgammon and chess allegedly designing their games according to each school). Despite this pervasive conflict, the author asserts that the sunni position (attributing all creation to God while affirming human acquisition) is stronger because of a single point: the premise that 'the contingent must have a cause' is essential for proving the existence of the Creator. If we reject it, we cannot prove God's existence. If we accept it, we must conclude that the servant is not independent in creating actions. Thus the proof for God's existence and the proof for the servant's non-independence are the same proof, making the sunni side stronger and more complete.

Key Terms
قدرية
جبرية
بديهة
حسن المدح والذم
الممكن لا بد له من سبب

Checkpoint

A) The contingent must have a cause.
B) The goodness of praise and blame.
C) The proof of knowledge.
D) The imperfection of the servant.

Key Takeaway
Both Jabariyya and Qadariyya base their positions on premises that are generally accepted as intuitive, yet these premises lead to contradictory conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you experienced a conflict between two 'obvious' truths in your own life? How did you respond—did you reject one or sit with the tension? This lesson shows that even great scholars acknowledged a conflict between two intuitive premises. Are you willing to hold onto paradoxes in your faith when both sides seem true?
?
The Qadariyya premise says that praise and blame only make sense if humans have real power. Do you live as if your choices truly matter, or do you sometimes use qadar as an excuse to avoid responsibility? Examine your heart: are you hiding behind God's decree to escape accountability for your actions?
?
The author presents both premises as intuitive. But do you actually feel the tension? When you think about your next decision, do you sense both that you are choosing and that God's will is over all? That lived tension is the point. In prayer, have you ever simultaneously thanked God for guidance and felt the weight of your own effort? Let that double awareness shape your worship today.

Rational Proofs: Knowledge vs. Intention
Author presents the rational proof of each sect and declares a conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proofs each sect uses: the proof of knowledge and the proof of intention, and how they conflict.

Core Idea
Jabariyya say: what we don't know can't be ours. Qadariyya say: what follows our intention is ours.

Beyond intuitive premises, both sides advance rational proofs based on epistemology and psychology. This lesson examines how each sect uses a reasoning about knowledge and intention to support their view of human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the rational proofs section. Notice the two arguments presented.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find the Jabariyya's 'proof of knowledge' (دليل العلم) and the Qadariyya's 'proof of intention' (دليل القصد). Understand each.
Jabariyya: we don't know the details of our actions, so they aren't ours. Qadariyya: our acts follow our intentions, so they are from us.
3
Analyze the conflict
Both proofs are rational yet arrive at opposite conclusions. Ask: which one seems more convincing to you, and why?

Perfection and Deficiency: Divine Attributes vs. Human Acts
Author presents the two perfection-based arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how arguments about divine perfection and human deficiency are used by both sects.

Core Idea
Jabariyya: creating is perfection, so only God creates. Qadariyya: evil acts are deficiency, so God cannot create them.

A further layer of the debate involves divine attributes: what befits God's perfection and what is beneath His majesty. Both sects weaponize the concept of perfection and deficiency to support their positions. This lesson presents their respective arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the part about perfection and deficiency. Note the single point (حرف واحد) each sect relies on.
2
Identify each sect's argument
Jabariyya: power to create is perfection, so it belongs only to God. Qadariyya: creating foolish acts would be deficiency in God.
Both use the same notion: what is befitting to God.
3
Recognize the symmetry
Both arguments are based on divine perfection but applied to opposite conclusions. See how each selects a different aspect of the acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القدرية : فقد عولوا في تقرير قولهم : على أن حسن المدح والذم معلوم بالبديهة. ونعلم بالبديهة : أنه لو لم يكن العبد قادراً على الفعل لما حسن المدح والذم . وما كان أصلا للبديهي : أولى أن يكون بديهياً . فالحاصل : أن عمدة الجبرية هي أن الممكن لا بد له من مرجع وعمدة القدرية : هي حسن المدح والذم . وهما مقدمتان معلومتان بالبديهة . فقد وقع التعارض بين هاتين المقدمتين في هذا المقام . وأما الدلائل العقلية : فاعتماد الجبرية على أن هذه التفاصيل ، غير معلومة لنا فلا تكون مخلوقة لنا . واعتماد القدرية على أن هذه الأفعال ، واقعة على وفق مقصودنا ودواعينا . فهي منا . فقد وقع التعارض بين دليل العلم ودليل القصد . وأما الإلزامات العائدة إلى باب الكمال والنقصان . فاعتماد الجبرية على حرف واحد ، وهو أن القدرة على الإيجاد صفة كمال ، فلا يليق بالعبد الذي هو منبع النقصان . واعتماد القدرية على حرف واحد ، وهو أن أفعال العباد سفه وعبث ، وإيجادها نقصان . وذلك لا يليق بالإله المتعالي عن النقصان . وأما الدلائل السمعية . فالقرآن مملوء مما يوهم الجبر تارة ، ومما يوهم القدر أخرى . وأما الأنام . فكذلك جميع الملل والنحل ، بعضهم مجبرة ، وبعضهم قدرية . ولا ترى أمة من الأمم خالية عن هاتين الطائفتين . وأما الأوضاع والحكايات . فهي أيضاً متعارضة في هذا الباب ، حتى قيل : إن واضع النرد وضعه على مذهب الجبر ، وواضع الشطرنج وضعه على مذهب القدر. فظهر عندك أن التعارض غالب في هذه المسألة إلا أن مذهبنا أقوى بسبب حرف واحد ، وهو أنا إن قدحنا في قول : الممكن لا بد له من سبب انسد علينا باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن سلمنا بهذه المقدمة لزمنا القول بأن العبد غير مستقل بالإيجاد . فلما كان دليل قولنا في هذه المسألة ، ودليل إثبات الصانع : دليلاً واحداً ، لا جرم كان جانبنا أقوى وأكمل .
As for the Qadariyya: they relied in establishing their statement on the fact that the goodness of praise and blame is known by intuition. And we know by intuition that if the servant were not capable of the act, then praise and blame would not be good. And what is the foundation of the intuitive is more worthy of being intuitive. So the conclusion: the mainstay of the Jabariyya is that the contingent must have a source, and the mainstay of the Qadariyya is the goodness of praise and blame. And they are two premises known by intuition. Thus conflict occurred between these two premises in this matter. As for rational proofs: the reliance of the Jabariyya is that these details are not known to us, so they are not created by us. And the reliance of the Qadariyya is that these acts occur in accordance with our intention and motives, so they are from us. Thus conflict occurred between the proof of knowledge and the proof of intention. As for the implications that revert to the matter of perfection and deficiency: the reliance of the Jabariyya is on one point, which is that power to bring into existence is an attribute of perfection, so it is not befitting the servant who is a source of deficiency. And the reliance of the Qadariyya is on one point, which is that the acts of servants are folly and frivolity, and bringing them into existence is deficiency, and that is not befitting the God exalted above deficiency. As for scriptural proofs: the Qur'an is filled with what suggests predestination at times and what suggests free will at others. As for the nations: likewise all religious communities and sects, some are Jabariyya, some are Qadariyya. And you do not see any nation that is free of these two groups. As for customs and stories: they also conflict in this matter, until it was said: the inventor of backgammon placed it according to the school of jabr, and the inventor of chess placed it according to the school of qadr. So it became clear to you that conflict is dominant in this issue, except that our school is stronger due to one point: that if we object to the statement 'the contingent must have a cause', the door to proving the Maker is blocked for us, and if we accept this premise, we are compelled to say that the servant is not independent in bringing into existence. So since the proof of our statement in this issue and the proof of establishing the Maker are one proof, inevitably our side is stronger and more complete.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a comparative analysis of the arguments between the Jabariyya (predestinarians) and the Qadariyya (free-will advocates) across multiple categories, concluding with the author's defense of the Ash'ari/sunni position. The author begins with the rational intuitive premises: the Jabariyya base their view on the principle that every contingent event requires a source (cause), while the Qadariyya base theirs on the intuitive moral judgment that praise and blame only apply to voluntary acts. These two intuitive premises conflict. Next, rational proofs: the Jabariyya argue that if the details of actions are not known to us, they cannot be created by us (implying they are from God); the Qadariyya argue that acts follow our intentions and motives, so they originate from us. This is a conflict between the proof of knowledge and the proof of intention. Then perfection and deficiency: the Jabariyya say power to create is a perfection, so it belongs only to God, not the deficient servant; the Qadariyya say that creating acts of folly would be a deficiency in God. Scriptural proofs: the Qur'an contains verses that seem to support both sides. Similarly, all nations contain both groups. Even customs and stories reflect the conflict (e.g., the inventors of backgammon and chess allegedly designing their games according to each school). Despite this pervasive conflict, the author asserts that the sunni position (attributing all creation to God while affirming human acquisition) is stronger because of a single point: the premise that 'the contingent must have a cause' is essential for proving the existence of the Creator. If we reject it, we cannot prove God's existence. If we accept it, we must conclude that the servant is not independent in creating actions. Thus the proof for God's existence and the proof for the servant's non-independence are the same proof, making the sunni side stronger and more complete.

Key Terms
كمال
نقصان
قدرة على الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the single point (حرف واحد) that the Jabariyya rely on regarding perfection?
A) Human acts are foolish.
B) Power to create is perfection.
C) God is beyond deficiency.
D) The servant is a source of deficiency.

Key Takeaway
Arguments about divine perfection and human deficiency are used symmetrically by both sects to support their respective positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own actions, do you see more evidence of your deficiency or your ability to create good? How does that affect your view of God's role in your deeds?
?
If you believe that all your actions are created by God, how does that impact your sense of responsibility for sin? Are you tempted to blame God for your shortcomings?
?
How do you reconcile the fact that you often do foolish or frivolous things with the belief that God is all-powerful and all-good? Does this passage challenge you to think deeper about your own accountability and dependence on God?

Divine Decree
beginner

Scriptural and Cultural Evidence: The Universal Conflict
Author presents the evidence from scripture, history, and culture showing the universal conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see that the conflict between jabr and qadr extends to scripture, history, and even everyday culture, showing its pervasiveness.

Core Idea
The Qur'an, all nations, and even games reflect the same division between jabr and qadr.

The disagreement is not limited to rational theology. Sacred scripture, historical communities, and cultural artifacts all bear witness to this persistent divide. This lesson surveys the evidence from these external domains.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Notice the three categories: scriptural proofs, nations, and customs/stories.
2
Examine the scriptural evidence
The Qur'an contains verses suggesting both predestination and free will. Reflect on why this might be.
3
Consider the nations and customs
All religious communities have both groups. Even games like backgammon and chess are said to represent the two doctrines.
4
Conclude the pervasiveness
The conflict is not accidental but deeply embedded in human thought and culture.

?
When you read the Qur'an, which type of verses—those emphasizing divine control or those emphasizing human responsibility—do you find more challenging to your daily life? What does that reveal about your heart's inclination?
?

?
Even games like backgammon and chess reflect the conflict. Think of an area in your life—work, relationships, or worship—where you act as if everything is predetermined or as if you are completely in control. Which area requires more trust in Allah's decree and which requires more personal effort? What one step can you take today to balance both?

Divine Decree

The Author's Resolution: One Crucial Point
Author presents the decisive argument that favors the sunni position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's decisive argument: that the premise 'the contingent must have a cause' is essential for proving God's existence, and accepting it logically entails that humans are not independent creators.

Core Idea
The same proof that establishes God's existence also refutes human independence.

After presenting the pervasive conflict across all domains, the author offers a resolution. He argues that the sunni (Ash'ari) position is uniquely strong because it anchors itself on a premise without which the existence of God cannot be proven. This lesson presents that decisive reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the final section where the author concludes. Note the 'one point' (حرف واحد) that gives his school strength.
2
Understand the key premise
The premise is 'the contingent must have a cause'. This is indispensable for proving the existence of the Creator.
3
See the implication
If we accept this premise, we must also accept that the servant is not independent in bringing acts into existence, because the true cause is God.
4
Grasp the unity of proof
The proof for God's existence and the proof for human non-independence are the same proof. This makes the sunni position coherent and stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Ash'ari position is arbitrary or that the conflict is irresolvable.
Correction: The author shows that the Ash'ari position is rationally necessary because it preserves the foundation for proving God's existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the sunni (Ash'ari) position stronger?
A) Because it has more scriptural support.
B) Because its proof for human non-independence is the same as the proof for God's existence.
C) Because it rejects the intuitive premise of the Qadariyya.
D) Because it avoids all contradictions.

Key Takeaway
The sunni position is not just one option among many; it is the only position that simultaneously upholds the rational basis for God's existence and resolves the debate on human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the very proof that establishes God’s existence also strips you of claiming independent power over your own actions, then who are you to boast about your achievements? In what area of your life do you still subtly believe you are the 'real cause' rather than God?
?
The author says the proof for God and the proof for human dependence are one and the same. Do you live as though your deeds originate from you or from God? How would your humility shift if you truly internalised that you are not the independent origin of any good you do?
?
Imagine you reject the premise that everything contingent must have a cause. You would then have no rational basis to believe in a Creator. Are you willing to sacrifice belief in God just to hold onto the illusion of your own independence? What will you choose when you realise the two are inseparable?

beginner
4 min
The Dilemma of Independent Power: Ali's First Story
The first story: a claimant of independent power is corrected by Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that claiming independent power over actions leads to either shirk or self-deification.

Core Idea
You cannot claim to own your actions without God without becoming a partner or a rival.

Ali ibn Abi Talib was once approached by a man who asserted that he personally possessed good and evil, obedience and disobedience. This story illustrates how a simple logical dilemma can expose the falsity of human autonomy in qadar. Let's examine Ali's response and the man's repentance.

Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the dialogue rhythm.
Pay attention to the direct speech.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Identify the two possible answers to Ali's question and their theological consequences.
Each answer leads to an unacceptable conclusion.
3
Reflect on the outcome
The man repents immediately. Consider what this teaches about the Islamic stance on human agency.
Repentance indicates acceptance of the truth.

ولتذكر الآن طرفاً من الحكايات المذكورة في هذه المسألة : الحكاية الأولى : جاء رجل إلى علي بن أبي طالب ، وقال : أنا أملك الخير والشر ، والطاعة والمعصية . فقال علي بن أبي طالب : تملكها مع الله أو تملكها بدون الله ؟ فإن قلت : أملكها مع الله فقد ادعيت أنك شريك الله ، وإن قلت : أملكها بدون الله ، فقد ادعيت أنك أنت الله . فتاب الرجل على يديه . الحكاية الثانية : جاء رجل آخر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ، وسأل عن القضاء والقدر . فقال : بحر عميق لا يدرك غوره ، فأعاد السائل السؤال . فقال : طريق مظلم لا نسلكه ، فأعاد السؤال . فقال علي : لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم : تعرف معناها ؟ قال : لا . قال : معناها : لا حول عن معصية الله إلا بعصمة الله . ولا قوة على طاعة الله إلا بتوفيق الله ، وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكرناه ، من أن القادر متساوي القدرة بالنسبة إلى الطاعة والمعصية . فلا يترجح أحد الجانبين على الآخر إلا لمرجح من قبل الله .
And now remember some of the stories mentioned in this issue: The first story: A man came to Ali ibn Abi Talib and said: I possess good and evil, obedience and disobedience. So Ali ibn Abi Talib said: Do you possess them with God or do you possess them without God? If you say: I possess them with God, then you claim to be God's partner; and if you say: I possess them without God, then you claim to be God Himself. So the man repented at his hands. The second story: Another man came to Ali ibn Abi Talib and asked about the decree and predestination. He said: A deep sea whose depth cannot be fathomed. The questioner repeated the question. He said: A dark path we do not traverse. The questioner repeated the question. So Ali said: 'There is no power and no strength except with God, the Most High, the Almighty'—do you know its meaning? He said: No. He said: Its meaning is: no turning from disobedience to God except by God's protection, and no strength for obedience to God except by God's success. And this refers to what we mentioned, that the one capable is equal in ability with respect to obedience and disobedience. So one of the two sides does not outweigh the other except by a preponderating factor from God.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents two stories (hikayat) from Ali ibn Abi Talib that illustrate the correct Islamic understanding of predestination and human agency. The first story addresses a man who claimed to possess good and evil independently. Ali traps him with a dilemma: if you possess them with God, you are claiming partnership with Him; if without God, you are claiming divinity. The man repents, realizing his error. The second story involves a man asking about qada and qadar. Ali initially discourages delving into the topic by calling it a deep sea and a dark path. When pressed, he explains the meaning of the phrase 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' as a key: no turning away from sin except by God's preservation ('isma), and no strength for obedience except by God's success (tawfiq). The author comments that this alludes to the earlier discussion (from the previous lesson) that the capable agent (the human) has equal ability towards obedience and disobedience, and only a preponderating factor from God tips the balance. Thus, human action is not independent but requires divine grace.

The Deep Sea of Qadar: Ali's Second Story
The second story: a questioner is taught through 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah'.

Core Idea
True strength to obey and avoid sin comes only from God.

In this second encounter, a man persistently asks about predestination. Ali initially warns of its depth but eventually elucidates the meaning of a familiar phrase. This story teaches us that the key to understanding qadar lies in recognizing that all power belongs to God.

The Ash'ari Position is Stronger Due to One Letter
Author argues that the Ash'ari position on human action is stronger because it shares its proof with the proof for God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari view on human action is strengthened by its logical connection to the proof for God's existence.

Core Idea
The proof for human dependence mirrors the proof for God's existence.

In this brief but powerful passage, the author argues that the Ash'ari school's position on human action is actually stronger than its rivals precisely because it relies on the same causal principle that proves God's existence. This clever argument shows that rejecting the causal necessity of a contingent leads to atheism, while accepting it leads to affirming that humans do not act independently. Let's unpack this dense argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses very concise language.
2
Identify the conditional dilemma
The passage presents two conditional statements: one rejecting the causal principle and one accepting it. Find each and note the consequences.
The phrases 'إن قدحنا' and 'إن سلمنا' mark the two branches.
3
Connect the proofs
The author claims that the proof for the Ash'ari position and the proof for God's existence are the same. Think about how accepting the causal principle leads to both conclusions.
This unity of proof is the 'one letter' that makes their side stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن مذهبنا أقوى بسبب حرف واحد ، وهو أنا [ إن(١) ] قدحنا في قول : ه الممكن لا بد له من سبب ) انسد علينا باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن سلمنا بهذه المقدمة لزمنا القول بأن العبد غير مستقل بالإيجاد . فلما كان دليل قولنا في هذه المسألة ، ودليل إثبات الصانع : دليلاً واحداً ، لا جرم كان جانبنـا أقـوى وأكمل .
That our school is stronger because of one letter, and it is that if we [if?] object to the statement: 'The contingent must have a cause'), the door to proving the Maker is blocked against us, and if we accept this premise, we must say that the servant is not independent in bringing about [actions]. Since the proof of our statement in this issue and the proof of proving the Maker are one proof, necessarily our side is stronger and more complete.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents a concise argument for the Ash'ari position on human action. The author claims that their school is stronger due to a single point (ḥarf). He explains that if they were to reject the causal principle that every contingent must have a cause, then the proof for God's existence would be blocked. Conversely, if they accept that principle, they must concede that human beings cannot independently create their actions, because that would require a separate creative power. Since the same proof (dālīl) that establishes God as the Maker also establishes that human actions are not self-generated, the Ash'ari position is both consistent and superior.

سبب
إثبات الصانع
عبد
استقلال
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is meant as a direct proof for qadar (divine decree) rather than a meta-argument about consistency.
Correction: The passage is not establishing qadar itself, but showing that the Ash'ari position is logically stronger because it coheres with the proof for God's existence.

Checkpoint

B) The point that the proof for human action and the proof for God's existence are the same
C) The assertion that the contingent must have a cause
D) The claim that the servant is independent in creation

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on human action is logically tied to the same causal principle that proves God's existence, making it consistent and strong.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ash'ari position ties your actions directly to your belief in God. Would you rather hold a view that feels comfortable but inconsistent, or one that challenges your pride and forces you to live in constant awareness of God's power? Which one are you actually living by?

Ali's Dilemma: With God or Without God?
Story of Ali ibn Abi Talib using a dilemma to refute a man claiming independent possession of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma Ali ibn Abi Talib used to refute the claim that humans independently possess actions, and how it affirms that all creation belongs to Allah.

Core Idea
Either with God (shirk) or without God (claiming divinity) — no third option.

This short story from Islamic tradition presents a powerful theological argument against the view that humans have independent power over their actions. Ali ibn Abi Talib confronts a man who claims to own good and evil, obedience and disobedience, and traps him in a logical dilemma that leaves no room for human autonomy separate from God. It is a vivid illustration of the Ash'ari position that all acts are from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the narrative flow.
Note the man's claim and Ali's immediate response.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Ali presents two possibilities: either with God or without God. Understand the consequence of each.
What happens if the man says 'with God'? What if 'without God'?
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this dilemma refutes the idea of human independence in actions. How does it preserve tawhid?
This connects to the broader Islamic doctrine of divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الأولى : جاء رجل إلى علي بن أبي طالب ، وقال : أنا أملك الخير والشر ، والطاعة والمعصية . فقال علي بن أبي طالب : تملكها مع الله أو لملكها بدون الله ؟ فإن قلت : أملكها مع الله فقد ادعيت أنك شريك الله ، وإن قلت : أملكها بدون الله ، فقد ادعيت أنك أنت الله . فتاب الرجل على یده
The first story: A man came to Ali ibn Abi Talib and said: 'I possess good and evil, obedience and disobedience.' Ali ibn Abi Talib said: 'Do you possess them with God or do you possess them without God? If you say: I possess them with God, then you have claimed that you are God's partner. And if you say: I possess them without God, then you have claimed that you are God.' So the man repented.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This is a story (ḥikāya) that illustrates a refutation of the claim that humans are independent creators of their actions. A man asserts that he owns good and evil, obedience and disobedience. Ali ibn Abi Talib presents a dilemma: either the man's possession of these actions is 'with God' (maʿa Allāh), meaning God shares in the creation, or it is 'without God' (bilā Allāh), meaning the man creates independently. If it is with God, then the man claims partnership (shirk) with God in creation. If it is without God, then the man claims to be God Himself, because only God is the independent creator. Both options are blasphemous, so the man repents. The story demonstrates the Ash'ari view that all actions are ultimately from God.

Correction: The argument is about the source and ownership of the power to create actions, not merely moral accountability.

Key Takeaway
The only way to avoid shirk and maintain tawhid is to affirm that all actions belong to Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ali's Explanation of 'La Hawla wa La Quwwata' and Qadr
Ali interprets 'La hawla wa la quwwata' to explain qadr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali's interpretation of 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' encapsulates the Ash'ari doctrine that human ability is neutral and only God's grace tips the scale toward obedience or avoidance of sin.

Core Idea
Human power is equal for good and evil; only God's grace tips the scale.

In this second story from Ali ibn Abi Talib, we see a profound teaching about divine decree (qadr). When asked directly, Ali first gives cryptic warnings about the depth of the topic. But when pressed, he unveils the meaning of a familiar phrase that many Muslims recite daily. His explanation reveals a key Ash'ari principle: that human ability is neutral, and it is only through God's protection and success that we can turn from sin or perform obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and notice Ali's initial reluctance
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe how Ali first describes qadr as a deep ocean and a dark path, discouraging inquiry. This sets the stage for his deeper teaching.
This reluctance is not a rejection of the question but a pedagogical strategy.
2
Examine Ali's definition of 'hawla' and 'quwwa'
Focus on the lines where Ali breaks down the phrase. He explains 'hawla' as turning from sin (only by God's protection) and 'quwwa' as power to obey (only by God's success). Notice how he assigns specific meanings to each word.
3
Connect his definition to the Ash'ari concept of equal capacity
The author's comment explicitly links Ali's explanation to the doctrine that the capable agent has equal power toward obedience and disobedience. Understand that without a preponderating factor from God, neither side would prevail.
This is the core of the Ash'ari view on human action.
4
Reflect on the implication for human dependency
Consider what this means for everyday life: our ability to avoid sin and do good is not from ourselves but from God's grace. This transforms the meaning of the phrase we often recite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الثانية : جاء رجل آخر إلى علي بن أبي طالب ، وسأل عن القضاء والقدر . فقال : بحر عميق. لا يدرك غوره ، فأعاد السائل السؤال . فقال : وطريق مظلم ، لا نسلكه ، فأعاد السؤال . فقال علي : لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم : تعرف معناها ؟ قال : لا . قال : فما معناها ؟ قال : ولا حول عن معصية الله ، إلا بعصمة الله . ولا قوة على طاعة الله ، إلا بتوفيق الله ، وهذا إشارة إلى ما ذكرناه ، من أن القادر متساوي القدرة بالنسبة إلى الطاعة والمعصية . فلا يترجح أحد الجانبين على الآخر ، إلا لمرجح من قبل الله .
The second story: Another man came to Ali ibn Abi Talib and asked about al-qada wa al-qadr (divine decree). He said: 'A deep ocean whose bottom cannot be reached.' The asker repeated the question. He said: 'A dark path that we do not traverse.' He repeated the question. Then Ali said: 'There is no might and no power except with Allah, the High, the Great. Do you know its meaning?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'What is its meaning?' He said: 'There is no turning away from disobedience to Allah except with Allah's protection, and there is no power to obey Allah except with Allah's success. And this is an indication to what we mentioned, that the capable [agent] has equal power with respect to obedience and disobedience. So neither side preponderates over the other except by a preponderating factor from Allah.'
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
In this second story, a man asks Ali about divine decree (qada wa qadr). Ali first gives cryptic answers—'a deep ocean' and 'a dark path'—discouraging inquiry. When pressed, he explains the meaning of the phrase 'lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh'. He interprets it as: there is no turning away (ḥawla) from sin except by God's protection (ʿiṣma), and no power (quwwa) to obey except by God's success (tawfīq). This is followed by an authorial comment that this illustrates the Ash'ari doctrine that the human agent (al-qādir) has equal ability for both obedience and disobedience, and the preponderance (tarjīḥ) comes from God alone. Thus, the story reinforces that human action is dependent on God's grace.

Key Terms

قدر
حول
قوة
عصمة
توفيق
قادر
تساوي القدرة
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans have independent ability to choose obedience or disobedience without divine intervention.
Correction: Ali explains that turning from sin requires God's protection, and performing obedience requires God's success; human power is neutral and needs a divine preponderating factor.

Checkpoint
According to Ali's explanation, what does the phrase 'lā ḥawla wa lā quwwata illā billāh' mean?
A) There is no power to move except by God's permission
B) There is no turning from sin except by God's protection, and no power to obey except by God's success
C) There is no strength and no energy except with God's help
D) There is no might and no authority except from God

Key Takeaway
Ali's explanation shows that our ability to avoid sin and do good is entirely dependent on God's protection and success, not our own strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' in your daily prayers, do you genuinely feel that your ability to avoid a single sin or perform a single good deed comes entirely from Allah, or do you subconsciously credit your own willpower? What would change in your day if you lived that truth moment by moment?
?
Ali described the topic of qadr as a dark path not to be traversed, yet he still gave an explanation when pressed. Where in your faith have you demanded to understand matters that Allah has kept beyond human grasp? Are you willing to submit to that darkness, or does your heart secretly rebel against it?
?
This lesson teaches that your capacity for good and evil is equal without Allah's grace. Think of a time you fell into sin or succeeded in obedience. Can you honestly trace that outcome back to your own effort or to Allah's protection and success? How does this reshape your humility and reliance on Him today?

Ja'far al-Sadiq Silences a Qadari with Surah al-Fatiha
Ja'far al-Sadiq uses Surah al-Fatiha to refute Qadari doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ja'far al-Sadiq used a single verse from Surah al-Fatiha to refute the Qadari belief in independent human action.

Core Idea
Reciting 'You alone we ask for help' contradicts claiming independence from Allah.

In this short but powerful anecdote, Ja'far al-Sadiq demonstrates that the Qur'an itself provides arguments against heretical views. By simply asking a Qadari to recite Surah al-Fatiha, he exposes a logical contradiction in the Qadari's own belief. This story illustrates the use of scripture in theological debate and the importance of subtle linguistic points.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow before interpreting.
Notice how the story sets up a debate scenario.
2
Identify the key verse and question
Find the verse 'Iyyāka na'budu wa iyyāka nasta'īn' (1:5) and note Ja'far's question: why ask for help if action is from you?
The word 'nasta'īn' (we ask for help) is the crux.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Think about why the Qadari was silenced. How does asking for help imply dependence on Allah?
This shows the consistency of Qur'anic teaching with sound theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الثالثة : ورد الشام معتزلي، فكتب هشام بن عبد الملك إلى أبي جعفر الباقر ، ليحضر ويتكلم معه . فقال إني كبير، والآن ابني جعفر ينوب عني في المناظرة. فجاء جعفر الصادق ، وقال للقدري : اقرأ الفاتحة . فقرأ فلها بلغ ( إياك نعبد ، وإياك نستعين ) قال له جعفر الصادق : على ماذا تستعين بالله ، وعندك : أن الفعل منك . وجميع ما يتعلق بالإقدار بالتمكين ، والألطاف قد حصلت وتمت ؟ فانقطع القدري بهذا الحرف الواحد
The third story: A Mu'tazili came to Sham, so Hisham ibn Abd al-Malik wrote to Abu Ja'far al-Baqir to come and speak with him. He said: 'I am old, and now my son Ja'far represents me in debate.' So Ja'far al-Sadiq came and said to the Qadari: 'Read al-Fatiha.' So he read, and when he reached [the verse] 'You alone we worship and You alone we ask for help' (1:5), Ja'far al-Sadiq said to him: 'For what do you ask help from Allah, while according to you, the action is from you, and everything related to enabling through empowerment and divine graces have been obtained and completed?' So the Qadari was silenced by this single point.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This third story recounts a debate between Ja'far al-Sadiq and a Qadari (a term often used for Mu'tazila who believe humans create their own actions). Ja'far asks the Qadari to recite al-Fatiha. When the Qadari recites 'Iyyāka na'budu wa iyyāka nasta'īn' ('You alone we worship and You alone we ask for help'), Ja'far seizes on the phrase 'nasta'īn' (we ask for help). He asks: why do you ask God for help if, according to your doctrine, the action (the worship) is entirely from you and the enabling factors (iqdār, tamkīn, alṭāf) are already complete? The implication is that seeking help acknowledges dependence on God. The Qadari is silenced by this one point (ḥarf).

Correction: The verse 'Iyyaka nasta'in' shows that even the act of worship requires Allah's help, contradicting the Qadari claim.

Abu Hanifa's Refutation of a Qadari: The Example of Speech Letters
Story: Abu Hanifa asks a Qadari about articulation of letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hanifa used a practical example about the articulation of speech letters to refute the Qadari claim that humans create their own actions.

Core Idea
If you cannot explain how you produce sounds, you cannot claim to create them.

This lesson presents a famous story from the debates between Abu Hanifa and the Qadariyya. Abu Hanifa asks a seemingly simple question about how we produce letters like ح and خ. The Qadari's inability to answer becomes a powerful argument that human creation of actions is impossible. This story illustrates a key Ash'ari principle: the act of creation belongs to Allah alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the dialogue. Notice the progression of questions.
2
Identify the argument
See how Abu Hanifa moves from the Qadari's ignorance of the mechanism to the conclusion that he cannot create the letters.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutes the Qadari belief that humans create their own actions. What does it imply about the nature of creation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الرابعة : جاء قدري ليتكلم مع أبي حنيفة . فقال أبو حنيفة : هل يمكنك التكلم بالحروف ؟ فقال نعم. فقال اذكر : فذكرها . فقال : أين مخرج الحاء والخاء ؟ وكم بينهما ؟ وأي شيء تفعل حتى تخرج الحاء ؟ وأي شيء تفعل حتى تخرج الخاء ؟ فقال : لا أعرف . فقال أبو حنيفة : من لا يعرف هذه الأشياء . فكيف يمكنه أن يخلقها ويوجدها ؟
The fourth story: A Qadari came to speak with Abu Hanifa. Abu Hanifa said: 'Are you able to speak with letters?' He said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Mention them.' So he mentioned them. He said: 'Where is the point of articulation of ḥ and kh? And how far is between them? And what do you do to produce ḥ? And what do you do to produce kh?' He said: 'I do not know.' So Abu Hanifa said: 'One who does not know these things, how can he create them and bring them into existence?'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
In this fourth story, Abu Hanifa uses a practical example to challenge a Qadari who claims that humans create their own actions. Abu Hanifa asks about the letters (ḥurūf) of speech, specifically the sounds ḥ (ح) and kh (خ). He asks about their points of articulation (makhraj), the distance between them, and the physical actions needed to produce them. The Qadari cannot answer. Abu Hanifa then argues that if a person does not even understand how these sounds are produced—sounds that he himself utters—how can he claim to create them and bring them into existence (yukhliqahā wa yūjiduhā)? The implication is that the power to create is beyond human knowledge and belongs to God alone.

Key Terms

مخرج
حاء
خاء
خلق
إيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans create their own actions independently of God
Correction: Abu Hanifa shows that humans do not even know how they produce speech sounds, so they cannot be creators of their actions. Creation requires perfect knowledge, which only Allah has.

Checkpoint

A) Alif and Bā
B) Ḥā and Khā
C) Sīn and Shīn
D) Dāl and Dhāl

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's argument from the articulation of letters demonstrates that humans lack knowledge of their own actions, proving that they cannot be the true creators; only Allah creates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak, do you ever pause to realize that you cannot explain how your own tongue, lips, and breath produce specific sounds? Does this ignorance humble you and remind you that your speech—and all your actions—are ultimately created by Allah? How can you cultivate this awareness in your daily conversations?
?
If you truly believed that every sound you utter is created by Allah and not by you, how would it change the way you speak about others? Would you be more careful, more grateful, or more aware of the power of words? What is one specific change you can make today in your speech based on this lesson?
?
The Qadari could not answer Abu Hanifa's simple questions about the letters he himself pronounced. How many of your own actions are you ignorant of their full details? Does this realization shake your sense of independence from Allah? What would it mean for you to sincerely say, 'I cannot create anything; all is from Allah'?

Amr ibn Ubayd's Prayer Reveals Mu'tazili Inconsistency
Fifth story: A man exposes the inconsistency in Amr ibn Ubayd's prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Amr ibn Ubayd's prayer exposes a logical contradiction in the Mu'tazili doctrine that God does not will evil.

Core Idea
If God did not will the theft, why trust His will for its return?

This passage presents a clever story that uses a practical scenario to highlight a deep theological flaw. Amr ibn Ubayd, a leading Mu'tazili, is asked to pray for the return of a stolen cloak. His prayer reveals an inconsistency in his own belief about divine will. Let's examine how the story works and what it teaches about the necessity of God's universal will.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to understand the narrative and the characters' actions.
2
Identify the contradiction
Notice how Amr's prayer acknowledges that Allah did not will the theft, yet he still asks for the return. The man points out the inconsistency: if God's will is ineffective for the theft, why rely on it for the return?
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument challenges the Mu'tazili position that God does not will evil. Does it make prayer futile? What does it imply about the nature of divine will?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الخامسة : جاء رجل إلى عمرو بن عبيد . وقال : أنت مستجاب الدعوة . وقد سرق ردائي. فادع الله أن يرد علي ردائي . فرفع يده وقال : اللهم إنك لم ترد أن يسرق ، وقد سرق . اللهم فرد عليه رداءه . فقال الرجل : امسك عن الدعاء. فإنه لم يرد أن يسرق ردائي وقد سرق ، فإذا أراد أن يرد علي ردائي ، لم يرده أيضاً
The fifth story: A man came to Amr ibn Ubayd and said: 'You are one whose prayers are answered. Someone has stolen my cloak. So pray to God to return my cloak to me.' So he raised his hand and said: 'O Allah, You did not intend for it to be stolen, and it has been stolen. O Allah, return his cloak to him.' Then the man said: 'Stop praying. For if He did not intend for my cloak to be stolen and it was stolen, then if He intends to return my cloak to me, He might not return it as well.'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This fifth story targets a figure of the Mu'tazila, Amr ibn Ubayd, who holds that God does not will evil actions because He is just. A man asks Amr to pray for the return of his stolen cloak. Amr prays saying, 'O Allah, You did not intend for it to be stolen, and it has been stolen. O Allah, return his cloak.' The man then points out the inconsistency: if God's will is not effective for the theft (since He did not will it but it happened), then why should God's will be effective for the return? The man says: 'If He did not intend... and it happened, then if He intends to return... He might not return it as well.' This exposes the Mu'tazili problem of divine will being inoperative against evil, which makes prayer for change futile.

Key Terms

دعاء
إرادة
سرقة
رد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story is against prayer itself.
Correction: It critiques the Mu'tazili denial of God's universal will, not the act of praying.

Checkpoint

A) Amr prayed for something impossible
B) If God's will did not prevent the theft, then His will may also not bring the return
C) The man did not believe in prayer
D) Amr should have blamed the thief instead

Key Takeaway
The story shows that denying God's will over evil leads to logical incoherence in prayer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This story exposes the incoherence of limiting God's will. Are there areas in your life where you implicitly limit God's will—perhaps by thinking certain sins or hardships are outside His plan—and how does that affect your trust in His sovereignty?

The Magian's Reductio ad Absurdum
A story where a Magian refutes Amr ibn Ubayd using his own logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Magian uses Amr ibn Ubayd's own Mu'tazili logic to argue that the devils are stronger than God, and why this is a reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
If God's will can be thwarted, He is not the strongest.

This story presents a clever rhetorical trap. Amr ibn Ubayd, a Mu'tazili scholar known for his belief in free will, tries to persuade a Magian to accept Islam. But when the Magian uses Amr's own concession about God's will being opposed by devils, he turns the argument on its head. The Magian's reasoning exposes the logical inconsistency in denying God's absolute will.

Guided Path
1
Read the story carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the dialogue and the key turns in the conversation.
Pay attention to the Magian's final conclusion.
2
Identify the Magian's argument
The Magian says: 'I am with the stronger partner.' Trace why he considers the devils stronger. What premise does he use?
He compares God's will and the devils' will in terms of actual outcome.
3
Understand the reductio
Amr's claim that God wills the Magian's Islam but devils prevent it leads to the absurd conclusion that devils are more powerful than God. This is a reductio ad absurdum of Amr's position.
The story does not endorse dualism; it uses it to show the flaw in Amr's theology.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider what this story teaches about the importance of affirming God's absolute will and power. How does the Magian's logic force a choice between two problematic views?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية السادسة : قيل لعمرو بن عبيد : هل الزمك أحد سؤالاً لم تقدر على جوابه ؟ قال : بلى . ركبت البحر ، وكان في السفينة مجوسي . فقلت له : لم لا تسلم ؟ فقال : لأن الله لم يرد إسلامي . فإذا أراد إسلامي أسلمت . فقلت للمجوسي : إن الله يريد إسلامك ، ولكن الشياطين لا يتركونك . فقال المجوسي : فأنا أكون مع الشريك الأغلب . لأن الله أراد شيئاً ، ولم يحصل مراده. والشيطان أرادوه ، وحصل . فالشيطان غالب . والكون مع الغالب ، أولى من الكون مع المغلوب .
The sixth story: It was said to Amr ibn Ubayd: 'Has anyone ever forced you with a question that you could not answer?' He said: 'Yes. I boarded a ship, and there was a Magian on the ship. I said to him: Why do you not accept Islam? He said: Because Allah has not willed my Islam. When He wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to the Magian: Allah wills your Islam, but the devils do not leave you. The Magian said: Then I am with the stronger partner. Because Allah willed something and His will was not realized, and the devils willed and it was realized. So the devils are dominant. Being with the dominant is better than being with the dominated.'
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This final complete story involves Amr ibn Ubayd being cornered by a Magian (Zoroastrian). Amr asks the Magian why he does not accept Islam. The Magian replies that God has not willed his Islam—a fatalistic argument that Amr, as a Mu'tazili, should reject because he believes in free will. Amr then makes a concession: he says that God does will the Magian's Islam, but the devils prevent him. The Magian seizes on this: he throws Amr's own theology back at him. He says that if God wills something but it does not happen, while the devils will something and it does happen, then the devils are the dominant (ghālib) partner. The Magian then says he will side with the stronger. This story highlights the absurdity of denying God's absolute will, because it leads to the conclusion that evil forces can overpower God.

Key Terms
مجوسي
إسلام
مشيئة
شريك
غالب
مغلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story endorses Zoroastrian dualism (two equal powers of good and evil).
Correction: The story uses dualism as a rhetorical device to reduce Amr's position to absurdity; it does not affirm that devils are actually equal to or stronger than God.

Checkpoint
What argument does the Magian use to embarrass Amr ibn Ubayd?
A) The devils are evil, so they should be worshiped
B) Since the devils achieve their will while God does not, the devils are the stronger partner
C) Islam is not the true religion
D) Prayer is useless

Key Takeaway
This story demonstrates how a seemingly minor concession about God's will can lead to the absurd conclusion that evil forces are more powerful than God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin or a failure by saying 'Allah hasn't willed it for me'? How does the Magian's initial excuse confront you?
?
If you truly believe that nothing occurs except by Allah's will, how does that reshape your reaction when your plans fail or when you are tested?
?
The Magian sided with what appeared dominant. Are there areas in your life where you follow the dominant culture or your own desires rather than submitting to Allah's command? What will you do about it today?

Anecdote of al-Sahib and al-Isfara'ini: The Limits of Human Agency
Narrative anecdote demonstrating limitation of human agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a narrative anecdote demonstrates that human control over actions is limited and contingent upon divine decree.

Core Idea
Human agency is real but limited — even a sneeze and a cat can interrupt our plans.

This lesson presents a vivid anecdote from the Ash'ari tradition that challenges the Mu'tazilite claim of absolute human autonomy. Through a simple demonstration by al-Sahib, we see that despite our conscious choices, unforeseen events beyond our will can overturn our actions. This story serves as a powerful reminder that ultimate agency belongs to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: al-Sahib's claim, his demonstration, and the interruption.
Observe the sequence of actions he claims control over.
2
Identify the key twist
Pinpoint the moment when al-Sahib's control is broken. What event changes the trajectory?
The sneeze and the cat are external factors.
3
Understand the theological implication
Reflect on how this anecdote supports the Ash'ari view that human power is limited and that God's will is supreme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسفرايني، من أصحابنا - فقال الصاحب : لا شك أن فاعل أفعالي أنا لا غيري . ورفع لقمة ووضعها في فمه. وقال : الرفع باختياري ، والوضع في الفم باختياري ، والمضغ باختياري ، والابتلاع باختياري . ثم اتفق في أثناء المضغ أن عطس ، فسقطت اللقمة ، ووثبت الهرة ، فأخذت اللقمة ، وأكلتها
Al-Isfara'ini, one of our companions - then al-Sahib said: 'There is no doubt that the doer of my actions is me, not other than me.' He raised a morsel and placed it in his mouth. He said: 'Lifting is by my choice, placing in the mouth by my choice, chewing by my choice, swallowing by my choice.' Then it happened during chewing that he sneezed, so the morsel fell, and the cat leaped, took the morsel, and ate it.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a narrative argument against the Mu'tazilite view of human autonomous agency. The anecdote begins with al-Sahib asserting that he is the sole doer of his actions, listing deliberate acts. However, during his demonstration, an involuntary sneeze causes the morsel to fall, and a cat takes it away. This illustrates that despite his claimed control, events outside his will (sneeze, cat) interrupt his action, showing that ultimate control lies with God. The account thus supports the Ash'ari position that human power is limited and contingent on divine will.

Key Terms
فاعل (doer)
اختيار (choice)
عطس (sneezed)
هرة (cat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The anecdote denies that humans have any choice or agency.
Correction: It actually affirms that humans have choice (al-Sahib claims control over deliberate actions) but shows that such control can be overridden by events not under human power, thereby demonstrating limitation not negation.

Checkpoint

A) He loses his voice
B) He sneezes and a cat takes the morsel
C) The morsel is too hot
D) He chokes on the morsel

Key Takeaway
Human agency is real but limited; ultimate control belongs to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make plans for tomorrow, do you truly acknowledge that a sneeze or a cat—let alone a major calamity—could undo them in an instant? What would change in your heart if you lived with that awareness today?
?
Think of a recent moment where your control was suddenly taken away—a small interruption or a big disappointment. Did you resent it, or did it remind you of your dependence on Allah? Will you choose to see the next interruption as a sign of His control?
?
Al-Sahib listed his voluntary actions with confidence. In what areas of your life are you most confident in your own ability, and how does this story challenge that confidence? Can you surrender that area to Allah right now, even in a single conscious thought?

The Eighth Anecdote: Power over Opposites
An anecdote illustrating the Mu'tazilite requirement of power over opposites and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that capability requires power over both opposites and how the Ash'ari response uses the fruit anecdote to refute it.

Core Idea
True power implies mastery over both opposites—something only God possesses.

This lesson presents a vivid anecdote from a debate between al-Sahib ibn Abbad and the Ash'ari theologian Abu Ishaq. By using a simple fruit-picking scenario, Abu Ishaq exposes a logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite definition of power. The argument becomes a cornerstone for the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Guided Path
1
Setting the scene
Read the anecdote and notice the setting: a garden, a fruit, a challenge. The question 'who separated this fruit?' seems simple, but the answer carries deep theological weight.
Pay attention to the dialogue format.
2
Identify the logical structure
The anecdote has three parts: the question, the paradoxical answer, and the author's explanation. The answer claims that the one who separated is the same one who can reattach. Understand the logic: if the servant has power to separate, he must be able to reattach.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Since reattachment is impossible, the conclusion is that the separation was not truly by the servant's power. Reflect on what this means for human agency and divine power.
Connect to the broader debate on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية الثامنة : وحكى أن الصاحب بن عباد، كان جالساً مع الأستاذ أبي اسحاق، في بستان، فمد يده إلى ثمرة وفصلها عن الشجرة. وقال: أيها الأستاذ من الذي فصل هذه الثمرة عن الشجرة؟ فقال الأستاذ أبي اسحاق: الذي فصل هذه الثمرة عن هذا الغصن، الثمرة عن هذا الغصن، هو الذي يقدر على أن يصلها به مرة أخرى. والمراد منه: أن القادر عند المعتزلة لا بد وأن يكون قادراً على الضدين. فلو كان العبد قادراً على الفصل، لوجب أن يكون قادراً على الوصل. وحيث لم يقدر على الوصل، وجب أن لا يقدر على الفصل.
The eighth anecdote: It is related that al-Sahib ibn Abbad was sitting with Master Abu Ishaq in an orchard. He extended his hand to a fruit and separated it from the tree. He said: 'O Master, who separated this fruit from the tree?' Master Abu Ishaq said: 'He who separated this fruit from this branch — the fruit from this branch — is the one who is able to reattach it to it again.' The meaning is: The capable (al-qadir) according to the Mu'tazila must necessarily be capable of opposites (al-diddayn). So if the servant were capable of separation, it would be necessary that he be capable of connection. And since he is not capable of connection, it must be that he is not capable of separation.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument against the Mu'tazilite definition of power (qudra). The anecdote begins with al-Sahib's challenge: who separated the fruit? Abu Ishaq's answer is paradoxical: the same one who separated it is able to reattach it. The author explains the underlying reasoning: the Mu'tazilites hold that a capable agent must be capable of both opposites (bringing existence and non-existence). Thus, if a person truly has power to separate, he must also have power to reunite. Since reuniting the fruit is impossible (it would require reversing the act), the original separation cannot be due to the servant's power; it is from God. This is an indirect proof for the Ash'ari view that God creates all acts, and human power is acquired (kasb).

Key Terms
قادر
ضدان
فصل
وصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites believe that a human being can have true power over an act without being able to perform its opposite.
Correction: The anecdote shows that if a human truly had power to separate the fruit, they would also have power to reattach it, which is impossible. Therefore, human 'power' is not independent—it is created by God.

Checkpoint
According to the anecdote, what does the Mu'tazilite view of capability require?
A) Ability to do one thing only
B) Ability to do both an act and its opposite
C) Ability to do only good acts
D) Ability to do only evil acts

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite requirement that a capable agent must be able to perform both an act and its opposite leads to a contradiction, showing that only God has true power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a simple act today, like picking a fruit or opening a door, do you ever pause to realize that you cannot undo that act in the same way? How does this limitation humble you before God, who alone can create and revert all things?
?
The Mu'tazilites demanded that true power include both opposites. In your own life, where have you assumed you have control only to discover you cannot reverse your decisions? What does that reveal about your dependence on God's grace?
?
If your ability to act is truly a gift from God and not an independent power, how should this change your attitude when you succeed or fail? Write one concrete way you will remind yourself of your reliance on Allah tomorrow.

The Exchange on Divine Action and Evil
A quick theological exchange contrasting Mu'tazilite and Ash'ari views on divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core difference between Mu'tazilite and Ash'ari views on divine will and evil as expressed in this succinct exchange.

Core Idea
God's transcendence from evil vs. God's absolute will over all that occurs.

This short anecdote from a historical encounter between two major theologians captures the essence of a long-standing debate. Abd al-Jabbar's statement emphasizes God's purity from evil, while al-Isfara'ini's retort affirms God's sovereign will. Let's examine their words and understand the theological positions behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the two lines of Arabic carefully. Note the immediate response. The exchange is brief but dense.
2
Identify each speaker's claim
Abd al-Jabbar says God is transcendent above obscenity. Al-Isfara'ini says nothing occurs in God's dominion except what He wills. Write down the core claim of each.
The word 'fahsha' refers to evil/obscenity.
3
Contrast the positions
Consider how each statement defines God's relationship to evil. One removes God from evil; the other places all events under His will.
This is a classic Mu'tazilite vs. Ash'ari divide.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If God wills all that happens, does that make Him responsible for evil? The Ash'ari response is yes, but with a deeper understanding of divine wisdom.
This leads to further study on theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية التاسعة : كان الأستاذ أبو اسحاق الإسفرايني، جالساً في دار الصاحب بن عباد، فدخل القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني، فلما رأى الأستاذ أبا اسحاق، قال في الحال : سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء . فقال الأستاذ في الحال: سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء .
The ninth anecdote: Master Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini was sitting in the house of al-Sahib ibn Abbad when Judge Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadhani entered. When he saw Master Abu Ishaq, he immediately said: 'Glory be to Him who is transcendent above obscenity (al-fahsha).' The Master immediately said: 'Glory be to Him from whose dominion nothing proceeds except what He wills.'
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This brief exchange encapsulates the core difference between Mu'tazilite and Ash'ari theology on the issue of evil. Abd al-Jabbar, a leading Mu'tazilite, declares God's transcendence from obscenity (fahsha), meaning God does not and cannot create evil acts. Abu Ishaq counters by affirming God's absolute sovereignty: nothing occurs in His kingdom except what He wills, implying that even evil acts are under divine will. The immediate response shows the Ash'ari commitment to God's all-encompassing will, even for actions that appear evil, while the Mu'tazilite position attempts to shield God from direct responsibility for evil.

Key Terms

C) God's mercy
D) God's justice

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite and Ash'ari positions on divine action and evil are succinctly contrasted: one emphasizes God's transcendence from evil, the other His all-encompassing will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a tragedy, which of these two statements comes more naturally to you: 'God is above evil' or 'Everything happens by God's will'? Honestly examine your heart—do you ever blame God for suffering? How does this anecdote challenge you to reconcile both truths?
?

?
The two scholars each began with 'Glory be to Him'. Is your first reaction to hardship praise or protest? What would it take for your heart to naturally exalt God in every circumstance, whether good or seemingly evil?

The Tenth Anecdote: Saying La Hawla During Adhan
The author cites the ruling to say 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' upon hearing the adhan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunna of saying 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' in response to the adhan and its theological significance as an acknowledgment of God's help.

Core Idea
The adhan response humbly admits that all power for worship comes from Allah.

In this passage, the author cites a common fiqh practice: when the mu'adhdhin says 'Hayya 'ala al-salah' and 'Hayya 'ala al-falah,' the listener should respond with 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah.' While this may seem like a simple formula, the author unpacks its theological weight, connecting it directly to the doctrine of human power and divine aid. This lesson explores both the ruling and its deeper meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get the literal sense first. Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation to understand the basic meaning.
2
Identify the ruling and its context
Note that the author says 'most jurists' consider it Sunna to say this phrase when hearing 'Hayya 'ala al-salah' and 'Hayya 'ala al-falah.' This is a practical act of worship.
3
Unpack the interpretation
The author explains the phrase as meaning 'no power to perform prayer or any worship except with God's help (i'ana) and success (tawfiq).' This is a theological gloss on the common formula.
4
Connect to broader doctrine
Understand why this fits the Ash'ari school: it emphasizes that all human ability is dependent on divine causation, opposing views that attribute independent power to humans.
This connects back to the theme of qadar (divine decree).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by saying 'we suffice with these anecdotes in this chapter,' signaling that these examples are enough to make the point. End with a pious phrase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكاية العاشرة : قال أكثر الفقهاء : إذا قال المؤذن : حي على الصلاة، حي على الفلاح. فالسنّة عند سماع هذه الكلمة : أن يقال : لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله . كأنه يقول : لا حول ولا قوة على أداء الصلاة ، وعلى أداء سائر العبادات، إلا بإعانة الله وبتوفيقه . وذلك صريح مذهبنا . ولنكتف بهذه الحكايات في هذا الباب . والله أعلم بالصواب

As if he says: 'There is no power and no strength to perform prayer, and to perform all other acts of worship, except with God's help and His granting of success.' That is the explicit [doctrine] of our school. And we suffice with these anecdotes in this chapter. And God knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents a fiqh ruling that connects back to the theological theme of human power. The author cites the practice of saying 'la hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' when hearing the adhan's call to prayer. He interprets the phrase as an acknowledgment that the ability to perform worship comes solely from God's aid (i'ana) and success (tawfiq). This is presented as the explicit doctrine of the Ash'ari school (madhhab). The author then concludes the collection of anecdotes, stating they suffice for the chapter, and ends with a pious phrase.
