Infinite Regress of Will and Critique of Abu al-Husayn
Author argues from infinite regress of will and criticizes Abu al-Husayn's inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof from infinite regress of will and how it reveals the self-contradiction in Abu al-Husayn's position.

Core Idea
The will for an action is itself unwilled, proving determinism; Abu al-Husayn's own premises lead to the same conclusion.

The author presents a third rational proof for determinism by reflecting on the nature of will. Then he exposes the peculiar contradiction of Abu al-Husayn, who simultaneously endorsed determinist premises yet insisted on Mu'tazilite free will. This lesson unpacks both the proof and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Reflect on the nature of will
Consider what we know when we examine our own will. The passage says our will for an action is not itself willed.
This is the starting point of the proof.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
If willing an action required willing that will, it would go on infinitely. Therefore, our will is not subject to our own choice.
3
Examine Abu al-Husayn's claims
He said action depends on motive and necessarily follows, which is determinism, yet he claimed the servant is the creator.
Note the inconsistency.
4
Consider the author's diagnosis
The author suggests Abu al-Husayn may have concealed his true view from his companions due to fear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنا إذا رجعنا إلى أنفسنا علمنا : أن إرادتنا للشيء لا تتوقف على إرادتنا لتلك الإرادة وإلا لزم التسلسل : بل نعلم بالضرورة : أنا إن شئنا أم أبينا فإنا نريد ذلك الفعل المخصوص ونعلم أنه متى حصلت تلك الإرادة بنا فالإنسان مضطر في صورة مختار . والعجب من أبي الحسين أنه خالف أصحابه في قولهم : الفعل لا يتوقف على الداعي وزعم : أن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي وزعم أن حصول الفعل عقيب الداعي واجب. والاعتراف بهاتين المقدمتين عين الاعتراف بالجبر . ثم إنه بعد ذلك بالغ في إثبات الاعتزال وزعم : أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكون العبد موجداً . والجمع بين هذين القولين عجيب . والأظهر : أنه لما بالغ في تقرير الجبر من حيث المعنى فلعله خاف من أصحابه فأراد إخفاء ذلك المذهب بهذه المبالغة . والله أعلم .
Third: When we examine ourselves, we know: that our will for a thing is not dependent on our willing that will; otherwise an infinite regress would follow. Rather we know necessarily that whether we wish or not, we will that specific action, and we know that when that will occurs in us, the human being is compelled in the form of one who chooses. And it is astonishing that Abu al-Husayn opposed his companions in their statement: 'Action does not depend on a motive,' and he claimed that action depends on a motive, and he claimed that the occurrence of action following the motive is necessary. And acknowledging these two premises is exactly acknowledging determinism. Then after that he went to excess in establishing Mu'tazilism and claimed that necessary knowledge is obtained that the servant is the creator. Combining these two statements is astonishing. And the most apparent: since he went to excess in establishing determinism from the standpoint of meaning, perhaps he feared his companions, so he wanted to conceal that doctrine by this exaggeration. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof and then criticizes Abu al-Husayn's inconsistency. The third proof argues that our will for an action is itself not willed; otherwise there would be an infinite regress. We necessarily know that we will specific actions whether we wish to or not, which implies that in terms of the occurrence of will, we are compelled while apparently choosing. Then the author points out Abu al-Husayn's self-contradiction: he claimed that action depends on motive and that it necessarily follows, which is determinism, yet he also claimed that there is necessary knowledge that the servant creates his actions. The author finds this combination astonishing and suggests that perhaps Abu al-Husayn, after strongly arguing for determinism, exaggerated his Mu'tazilite claims to conceal his true stance from his companions.

Key Terms
تسلسل
داعي
جبر
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think determinism contradicts intuitive knowledge of free will, or that Abu al-Husayn's position is logically consistent.
Correction: The author shows that self-reflection reveals the will is not chosen, proving determinism, and that Abu al-Husayn's own premises lead to determinism, making his Mu'tazilite claims inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author point out in Abu al-Husayn's position?
A) He claimed actions are created by God but also by the servant
B) He claimed action depends on motive and necessarily follows (determinism) yet also claimed necessary knowledge of the servant as creator
C) He opposed the Mu'tazila but also agreed with them
D) He argued for free will but also for determinism

Key Takeaway
The will for an action is not willed; this proves determinism, and Abu al-Husayn's own premises expose his internal contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own will, can you honestly say that every desire you have for an action was willed by you? How does the author's argument about the unwilled nature of will make you reconsider the origins of your own inclinations? Will you begin to trace your desires back to their source and ask Allah for sincerity?
?
The author says we are 'compelled in the form of one who chooses'. Have you ever experienced that tension—feeling fully responsible for your choices yet sensing forces beyond your control? How does this affect your view of your own accountability before Allah? Will you now more humbly acknowledge your dependence on Him in every action?
?
Abu al-Husayn apparently feared his companions and exaggerated his Mu'tazilite claims to conceal his determinist leanings. Are there any beliefs you hold deep down but hide or downplay because of fear of people's reactions? Will you commit to seeking truth regardless of social pressure, trusting that Allah knows your heart?

The Prophetic Consensus: Commands Prove Human Agency
Presents the second argument from prophetic commands and the implication of capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the prophets on divine commands implies that humans create their own actions.

Core Idea
Divine commands logically require the commanded person to be capable of acting.

This lesson unpacks the second argument in a series refuting determinism. The prophets unanimously taught that God commands and prohibits. If humans were not the agents of their own deeds, these commands would be meaningless — a fundamental absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the argument begins with the consensus of the prophets.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument's core is that commanding implies the capability of the commanded. Identify this logical connection.
Think about what it means to command someone who cannot obey.
3
Consider the reductio
The author uses an extreme example to show the absurdity of commanding an incapable being. Reflect on why this strengthens the argument.
Note the role of rational intuition (badihah) in rejecting the absurd case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الأنبياء عليهم السلام أجمعوا على أن الله أمر عباده ببعض الأشياء ، ونهاهم عن بعضها . ولو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لما صح ذلك . فكيف يعقل أن يقول الله للعبد : افعل الإيمان والصلوات والعبادات ، ولا تفعل الكفر والمعاصي. مع أن الفاعل لهذه الأفعال ، والتارك لها ليس هو العبد ؟ فإن أمر الغير بالفعل يتضمن الإخبار عن كون ذلك المأمور قادراً على الفعل ، حتى إنه لو لم يكن المأمور قادراً عليه المرض أو لسبب آخر ، ثم أمره غيره به. فإن العقلاء يتعجبون منه وينسبونه إلى الحماقة والجنون . ويقولون : إنك تعلم أنه لا يقدر على ذلك الفعل ، فكيف تأمره به ؟ ولو صح هذا لصح أن يبعث الله رسولاً إلى الجمادات مع المعجزات ، والكتب والشرائع . لأجل أن يبلغ ذلك الرسول تلك الشرائع إلى تلك الجمادات . ثم إنه تعالى يخلق الحياة والعقل لتلك الجمادات ، ويعاقبها ، لأجل أنهم لم يمتثلوا أمر الرسول ، حال كونها جمادات . وذلك مما يعلم فساده ببديهة العقل واعلم : أنا قد حكينا لهم استدلالهم بما تقرر من هذا الوجه ، على أن علم العبد بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه : علم ضروري . وذلك الوجه مغاير لهذا الوجه . لأنا في ذلك الوجه استدللنا بحصول العلم الضروري ، ويحسن المدح والذم على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكون العبد موجداً . والآن نستدل يحسن المدح والذم على كون العبد موجداً . فظهر الفرق
The second argument: The prophets, peace be upon them, agreed that God commanded His servants to some things and forbade them from some things. And if the servant were not the creator (mujid) of his own actions, that would not be valid. So how can it be understood that God says to the servant: 'Do faith, prayers, and acts of worship, and do not do disbelief and sins,' while the doer of these actions and the abandoner of them is not the servant? For commanding another to do an action entails informing that the commanded person is capable of the action, such that if the commanded person were not capable of it due to illness or another reason, and then another commanded him to do it, the rational people would wonder at him and attribute him to foolishness and madness. And they would say: 'You know that he is not able to do that action, so how do you command him to do it?' And if this were valid, it would be valid for God to send a messenger to inanimate objects with miracles, books, and laws, so that the messenger would convey those laws to those inanimate objects. Then He, exalted, would create life and intellect for those inanimate objects and punish them because they did not obey the messenger while they were inanimate. And that is known to be false by the primary intellect (badihah al-'aql). And know: We have narrated to them their argument based on what has been established from this aspect, that the servant's knowledge of being the creator of his own actions is necessary knowledge. And that aspect is different from this aspect. For in that aspect we argued by the occurrence of necessary knowledge, and the validity of praise and blame, that necessary knowledge occurs that the servant is the creator. And now we argue by the validity of praise and blame that the servant is the creator. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument in a series refuting the view that God creates all human actions, arguing instead that the servant is the creator (mujid) of his own actions. The proof is based on the consensus of the prophets (ijma' al-anbiya') that God issues commands and prohibitions. The author argues that commanding someone to do something logically implies that the commanded person has the ability (qudra) to perform the act. If the servant were not the one who brings his actions into existence, then divine commands would be invalid because they would be commanding an incapable subject. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if commanding an incapable person were valid, then God could send a messenger to inanimate objects, then later create life and reason in them and punish them for not obeying while they were inanimate—which is clearly absurd. After presenting this argument, the author clarifies the difference between this argument and a previous one (which used necessary knowledge). Here the proof relies on the validity of praise and blame (husn al-madh wa al-dhamm), which is a well-known ethical principle. The conclusion is that the servant must be the creator of his own actions to make sense of divine commands.

Key Terms
حجة
أمر
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine commands can be given even if humans have no power to act, as a test or formality.
Correction: This argument shows that commanding an incapable being is irrational, as rational people would consider it foolish. Thus, commands necessarily imply capability.

Checkpoint
What does the act of commanding imply about the person being commanded?
A) That the commander is powerful
B) That the commanded person is capable of performing the act
C) That the commanded person is already doing the act
D) That the commander is wise

Key Takeaway
Divine commands from the prophets logically prove that humans are the creators of their own actions, because commanding requires capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that God only commands what you are capable of. What command of God have you been treating as impossible in your life? Will you honestly confess that and take one concrete step toward obeying it this week?
?
When you sin, do you ever say, 'I couldn't help it'? How does this lesson strip away that excuse? What will you change about how you repent—not just asking forgiveness but taking responsibility for your choice?
?
The prophets' consensus proves that every command is a declaration of your ability. As you read the Qur'an this week, will you receive each command as a gift of capability from God? What will you do to remind yourself daily that you can obey because He has commanded?

Reductio ad Absurdum and the Basis of the Argument
Presents the reductio ad absurdum and the distinction from the previous argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a reductio ad absurdum to solidify the argument and how this argument differs from the previous one based on necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
If commanding an incapable agent were valid, even inanimate objects could be accountable.

This lesson continues the second argument. After establishing that commands imply capability, the author presents a striking counterexample: if it were valid to command an incapable being, then God could send messengers to rocks and punish them for disobedience. This absurdity reinforces the original point. The lesson also clarifies how this argument differs from a previous one.

Guided Path
1
Follow the reductio
Read the passage where the author imagines God sending a messenger to inanimate objects, then later punishing them. See how this leads to an absurd conclusion.
This is a standard logical tool: if a premise leads to absurdity, the premise is false.
2
Understand the distinction
The author notes that this argument differs from a previous one. The previous argument relied on necessary knowledge (ʻilm ḍarūrī); this one relies on the validity of praise and blame (ḥusn al-madh wa al-dhamm).
Praise and blame are ethical judgments; they only make sense if the agent is free.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that humans must be creators of their actions. Consider how this fits into the broader debate on predestination.

الحجة الثانية : الأنبياء عليهم السلام أجمعوا على أن الله أمر عباده ببعض الأشياء ، ونهاهم عن بعضها . ولو لم يكن العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لما صح ذلك . فكيف يعقل أن يقول الله للعبد : افعل الإيمان والصلوات والعبادات ، ولا تفعل الكفر والمعاصي. مع أن الفاعل لهذه الأفعال ، والتارك لها ليس هو العبد ؟ فإن أمر الغير بالفعل يتضمن الإخبار عن كون ذلك المأمور قادراً على الفعل ، حتى إنه لو لم يكن المأمور قادراً عليه المرض أو لسبب آخر ، ثم أمره غيره به. فإن العقلاء يتعجبون منه وينسبونه إلى الحماقة والجنون . ويقولون : إنك تعلم أنه لا يقدر على ذلك الفعل ، فكيف تأمره به ؟ ولو صح هذا لصح أن يبعث الله رسولاً إلى الجمادات مع المعجزات ، والكتب والشرائع . لأجل أن يبلغ ذلك الرسول تلك الشرائع إلى تلك الجمادات . ثم إنه تعالى يخلق الحياة والعقل لتلك الجمادات ، ويعاقبها ، لأجل أنهم لم يمتثلوا أمر الرسول ، حال كونها جمادات . وذلك مما يعلم فساده ببديهة العقل واعلم : أنا قد حكينا لهم استدلالهم بما تقرر من هذا الوجه ، على أن علم العبد بكونه موجداً لأفعال نفسه : علم ضروري . وذلك الوجه مغاير لهذا الوجه . لأنا في ذلك الوجه استدللنا بحصول العلم الضروري ، ويحسن المدح والذم على أن العلم الضروري حاصل بكون العبد موجداً . والآن نستدل يحسن المدح والذم على كون العبد موجداً . فظهر الفرق
The second argument: The prophets, peace be upon them, agreed that God commanded His servants to some things and forbade them from some things. And if the servant were not the creator (mujid) of his own actions, that would not be valid. So how can it be understood that God says to the servant: 'Do faith, prayers, and acts of worship, and do not do disbelief and sins,' while the doer of these actions and the abandoner of them is not the servant? For commanding another to do an action entails informing that the commanded person is capable of the action, such that if the commanded person were not capable of it due to illness or another reason, and then another commanded him to do it, the rational people would wonder at him and attribute him to foolishness and madness. And they would say: 'You know that he is not able to do that action, so how do you command him to do it?' And if this were valid, it would be valid for God to send a messenger to inanimate objects with miracles, books, and laws, so that the messenger would convey those laws to those inanimate objects. Then He, exalted, would create life and intellect for those inanimate objects and punish them because they did not obey the messenger while they were inanimate. And that is known to be false by the primary intellect (badihah al-'aql). And know: We have narrated to them their argument based on what has been established from this aspect, that the servant's knowledge of being the creator of his own actions is necessary knowledge. And that aspect is different from this aspect. For in that aspect we argued by the occurrence of necessary knowledge, and the validity of praise and blame, that necessary knowledge occurs that the servant is the creator. And now we argue by the validity of praise and blame that the servant is the creator. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument in a series refuting the view that God creates all human actions, arguing instead that the servant is the creator (mujid) of his own actions. The proof is based on the consensus of the prophets (ijma' al-anbiya') that God issues commands and prohibitions. The author argues that commanding someone to do something logically implies that the commanded person has the ability (qudra) to perform the act. If the servant were not the one who brings his actions into existence, then divine commands would be invalid because they would be commanding an incapable subject. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if commanding an incapable person were valid, then God could send a messenger to inanimate objects, then later create life and reason in them and punish them for not obeying while they were inanimate—which is clearly absurd. After presenting this argument, the author clarifies the difference between this argument and a previous one (which used necessary knowledge). Here the proof relies on the validity of praise and blame (husn al-madh wa al-dhamm), which is a well-known ethical principle. The conclusion is that the servant must be the creator of his own actions to make sense of divine commands.

Key Takeaway
The absurdity of commanding inanimate objects shows that commands require capability, and this argument is distinct from one based on necessary knowledge.

The Impossibility of Kasb as a Middle Ground
Refutes the objection that acquisition (kasb) suffices for praise and blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of acquisition (kasb) fails to resolve the problem of human action and how a logical dichotomy exposes its impossibility.

Core Idea
There is no middle ground: an action either comes from the servant or it does not.

In this passage, the author anticipates a common objection from Ash'ari theology—that even if humans do not create their actions, they 'acquire' them, and this suffices for moral responsibility. The author responds with a rigorous logical dichotomy that leaves no room for a third concept like kasb to solve the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the objection that kasb might suffice for praise and blame.
The objector suggests a middle ground between human creation and divine creation.
2
Analyze the dichotomy
The author presents a strict either-or: the action either comes from the servant or it does not. Identify why this dichotomy eliminates kasb as a viable option.
There is no third possibility (la wasitah baynahuma).
3
Understand the consequences
If the action comes from the servant, the purpose is achieved. If not, commanding it becomes impossible. Reflect on why this renders kasb ineffective.
The term 'kasb' does not change the logical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : العبد وإن لم يكن موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، إلا أنه مكتسب لها ويكفي (٣) هذا القدر في حسن المدح والذم ؟ لأنا نقول : دخول فعل العبد في الوجود ، إما أن يكون من العبد ، وإما أن لا يكون منه . وهذا تقسيم دائر بين النفي وبين الإثبات ، ولا واسطة بينهما . فإن كان فعل العبد في الوجود من العبد ، فقد حصل المقصود . وإن لم يكن به ، فحينئذ يكون أمره بتحصيل العقل أنه لا قدرة له على التحصيل يكون أمراً له لما ليس في وسعه. وحينئذ تتوجه المحالات المذكورة. وإذا كان ترتيب الدليل على هذا الوجه لم يكن إلقاء لفظ الكسب (۱) في البين : دافعاً لهذا الإشكال البتة
And it cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say: The servant, even if he is not the creator of his own actions, nevertheless he acquires (muktasib) them, and this amount suffices for the validity of praise and blame? For we say: The entry of the servant's action into existence is either from the servant or not from him. And this is a division that alternates between negation and affirmation, and there is no intermediary (wasitah) between them. For if the servant's action in existence is from the servant, then the purpose is achieved. And if it is not from him, then his commanding him to acquire it, while he has no capability to acquire it, is commanding him to what is not within his capacity. And then the aforementioned impossibilities follow. And if the arrangement of the proof is in this manner, the throwing of the term acquisition (kasb) into the matter does not ward off this problem at all.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection to the previous argument, namely the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb). The objector suggests that even if the servant is not the direct creator of his actions, he 'acquires' them, and this is sufficient for praise and blame. The author refutes this by pointing out a logical dichotomy: the servant's action, when it comes into existence, either comes from the servant or not; there is no middle ground (la wasitah baynahuma). If it comes from the servant, then the point is conceded. If it does not, then commanding the servant to acquire it, while he has no power to bring it about, is commanding the impossible, which leads to the same absurdities mentioned earlier (like commanding inanimate objects). Therefore, the mere use of the word 'kasb' does not solve the problem. The refutation is rigorous: it forces the opponent to either accept full human agency or face the consequences of divine command without human capability.

واسطة

Misconception: Some think that the concept of acquisition (kasb) provides a valid middle ground between human creation and divine creation, resolving the problem of moral responsibility.
Correction: The author shows that there is no logical middle ground: the action either comes from the servant or it does not. Kasb does not change this dichotomy, so it cannot solve the problem.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Acquisition (kasb) does not provide a genuine middle ground; without true agency, human action cannot be the basis for meaningful command.

The Third Argument: Taklif Requires Human Power
Proves that if God created actions, taklif would be impossible, which is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third argument against the view that God creates all human actions, including the principle that acquiring what already exists is impossible (tahsil al-hasil muhal) and the appeal to self-evident primary intellect (badihah al-'aql).

Core Idea
Without real human power, taklif is impossible.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate about human free will. The author uses a logical proof to show that if God were the sole creator of all actions, then God's command to humans would be impossible. This argument appeals to self-evident intuition about our ability to choose between actions. By examining this proof, we see how kalam thinkers used reason to defend human moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Note the overall structure: a conditional statement followed by an explanation of the entailment and a final appeal to self-evidence.
The passage is a single, continuous argument.
2
Identify the conditional and its consequence
The argument begins: 'If God were the creator of the servant's action, then the servant would be tasked with what he has no power over.' This is a conditional. The consequent is claimed to be false, so the antecedent must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Explore the two disjuncts in the entailment
The author examines two possibilities for the servant's power: either he has it at the time God creates the act, or at the time God does not create it. Both are shown to be impossible.
The first leads to 'tahsil al-hasil' (acquiring what already exists, which is impossible). The second would imply the servant has creative power, contradicting the premise.
4
Appreciate the appeal to primary intellect
After showing that the servant would have no power if God creates all actions, the author points to our self-evident ability to move right or left, distinguishing it from creating celestial bodies. This shows we have real power over our actions.
This appeal to badihah al-'aql is meant to be undeniable.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that the original claim (God creates all actions) is false because it leads to an absurd consequence. This preserves the reality of human power and moral responsibility.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core proof is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالله التوفيق . الحجة الثالثة : لو كان موجد فعل العبد هو الله تعالى ، لكان تكليف العبد بالفعل والترك ، تكليفاً بما لا قدرة له عليه ، واللازم باطل . فالملزوم مثله . بيان الملازمة : أن العبد [ لو كان (۲) ] له قدرة [ على الفعل (٣) ] لكان إما أن يقدر عليه حال ما خلق الله الفعل ، أو حال (4) ما لم يخلقه الله . والأول محال . لأن ذلك الفعل لما دخل في الوجود بتخليق الله تعالى ، امتنع أن يقدر العبد على تحصيله . لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال . والثاني محال . لأنه تعالى إذا لم يخلق ذلك الفعل ، فلو قدر العبد على تحصيله كان هذا تسليماً ، لكون العبد قادراً على التحصيل والإيجاد ، وذلك ينافي قولنا : إنه غير قادر عليه ، فثبت : أن غيره قادر عليه . فثبت (٥) ] أن موجد فعل العبد لو كان هو الله تعالى لما كان العبد قادراً على الفعل البتة . وإنما [ قلنا (٦) ] أنه قادر على الفعل . لأنه إذا قيل لنا : انتقل من هذا المكان إلى ذلك المكان ، ثم قيل لنا : اخلق شماً وقمراً وسماء وأرضاً . فإنا ببديهة العقل ندرك التفرقة بين البابين ، ونعلم بالضرورة : أنه يمكننا الحركة يمنة ، بدلاً عن يسرة ، وبالعكس . ولا
And by God is success. The third argument: If the creator of the servant's action were God Almighty, then the servant's being tasked (taklif) with action and abandonment would be tasking him with what he has no power over, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. Explanation of the entailment: That the servant, if he had power over the action, then either he would have power over it at the time when God creates the action, or at the time when God does not create it. The first is impossible because when that action comes into existence by God's creation, it is impossible for the servant to have power to acquire it, because acquiring what already exists (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible. And the second is impossible because if God does not create that action, and the servant had power to acquire it, this would be a concession that the servant is capable of acquisition and creation, and that contradicts our statement that he is not capable of it. So it is established that something else is capable of it. So it is established that if the creator of the servant's action were God Almighty, the servant would have no power over the action at all. Rather we say that he is capable of the action because when it is said to us: 'Move from this place to that place,' and then it is said to us: 'Create a sun and a moon and a sky and an earth,' we, by the primary intellect, perceive the distinction between the two cases, and we know necessarily that we can move to the right instead of the left, and vice versa. And not...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument, which is a logical proof against the position that God creates all human actions. The argument is structured as a conditional syllogism: if God were the creator of the servant's action, then the servant would be tasked with something impossible (taklif ma la yutaq), which is false; therefore the antecedent is false. The author explains the entailment by examining the servant's power (qudra). The servant either has power at the time God creates the act or at the time He does not. Both possibilities are impossible: if God creates the act, the servant cannot acquire what already exists (tahsil al-hasil muhal); if God does not create the act and the servant could acquire it, that would mean the servant has creative power, which contradicts the original claim. Thus, the servant could have no power at all if God were the creator. Yet we know by primary intellect (badihah al-'aql) that we have power, as seen in the clear distinction between moving to the right versus left and creating celestial bodies. The argument ends mid-sentence (likely continuing on the next page), but the core proof is complete: human power is self-evident and incompatible with the view that God creates all actions.

Key Terms

قدرة
تحصيل الحاصل
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it assumes what it wants to prove.
Correction: It is a reductio ad absurdum, not circular. It assumes the premise (God creates all actions) temporarily and shows it leads to a contradiction (taklif impossible). Since the consequence is false (we have taklif), the premise is false.

Checkpoint

A) The servant would have unlimited power.

C) The servant would be independent of God.
D) The servant would not exist.

Key Takeaway
The third argument proves that if God were the sole creator of actions, taklif would be impossible, which is false, therefore God is not the sole creator; human power is real and necessary for moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The argument uses the primary intellect to distinguish between moving your hand and creating a sun. When you sin, do you feel the weight of your own choice, or do you excuse yourself by saying 'it was written'? What would change if you started every day affirming: 'I have power, and I am accountable'?

The Failure of Kasb as a Middle Ground
Refutation of kasb via binary logical alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the theory of kasb is rejected as an invalid middle ground between divine creation and human power.

Core Idea
Kasb is a meaningless word that solves nothing in the debate on human action.

In this passage, the author presents a sharp critique of the concept of kasb (acquisition), which some theologians proposed as a middle ground between God's creation of all actions and human free will. He argues that either a person has genuine power to create his actions or he does not—there is no third option. The term 'kasb' is thus exposed as a vague and useless insertion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's bold initial claim and the logical structure that follows.
The author uses an exaggerated example (creating the sun and moon) to make a point about human capability.
2
Identify the two conditional branches
The author presents two possibilities: either the servant is capable of acquisition (تحصيل) or not. Trace each branch and see what conclusion follows.
The conclusion of both branches leads to the same outcome: kasb is unnecessary.
3
Reflect on the dismissal of kasb
Consider why the author calls kasb an 'unknown word' (لفظة مجهولة). How does this critique undermine the theory?
The author argues that inserting a term without clear meaning does not resolve the theological problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكننا خلق الشمس والقمر والسماء والأرض . ولولا كوننا قادرين على أفعالنا ، وإلا لما حصلت هذه التفرقة . وأما إلقاء لفظ الكسب في البين ، فلا فائدة فيه . لأنه إن كان قادرا على التحصيل ، فقد حصل المطلوب . وإن لم يكن قادراً عليه ، فالإلزام المذكور لازم ، ولا واسطة بين القسمين . فكيف ينفع في مثل هذا الموضع القاء لفظة مجهولة في البين ؟
We can create the sun and the moon and the sky and the earth. And were we not capable of our actions, then this differentiation would not have occurred. And as for inserting the word 'kasb' (كسب) into the matter, there is no benefit in it. Because if he is capable of acquisition (التحصيل), then the desired result has been obtained. And if he is not capable of it, then the mentioned proof is necessary, and there is no middle ground between the two divisions. So how can the insertion of an unknown word be beneficial in such a place?
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the theory of kasb (acquisition) as a middle ground between divine creation and human power. The author begins by asserting that we (humans) have the ability to create things like the sun, moon, sky, and earth—an obvious exaggeration to make the point that if we were not capable of our actions, we could not differentiate between our actions and God's. He then attacks the concept of kasb: if the servant is capable of acquisition (i.e., earning the act), then the desired result (human power) is already established; if he is not capable, then the argument that God alone creates actions stands, and there is no intermediate state. Therefore, inserting the vague term 'kasb' does not resolve the theological problem.

Misconception: Kasb is a valid middle ground between divine creation and human power.
Correction: The author shows that there is no middle ground: either the servant has power to create actions or not; kasb is an undefined term that does not resolve the dilemma.

Key Takeaway
The theory of kasb fails because it introduces a meaningless middle term that does not solve the problem of human power; if humans have power, the desired result is achieved; if not, the argument that God creates all actions stands.
